Will it also say whether it considers that the bans on peaceful demonstrations, the obstruction of freedom of expression and the use of preventive custody constitute violations of basic democratic principles and whether it intends to inform the Turkish authorities of its opposition to such measures?

 
 

Question no 40 by Efstratios Korakas (H-0263/02)
 Subject: Continuing violations of fundamental democratic and human rights in Turkey
 

The situation in Turkey has not only not improved (as some people have announced), it is actually deteriorating. In the space of a few days the Turkish government has banned peaceful demonstrations and arrested people as a preventive measure, the death toll from a hunger strike among political prisoners protesting about the 'white cells' has reached 89 and there has been bloodshed at the celebrations marking the Kurdish New Year, with many people injured and arrested. In addition a delegation of 200 Kurds who had set out from the countries of western Europe to hand to the Turkish authorities a demand by the Kurdish parliament in exile to permit the use of the Kurdish language has been prevented from crossing the borders of Bulgaria and Romania.

Will the Council say what action it has taken so far and what specific measures it intends to take in future to prevail upon the Turkish authorities finally to respect fundamental democratic and human rights and avoid sacrifices of innocent victims who are only claiming their fundamental rights, and whether it intends to ask Bulgaria and Romania, which are candidate countries, why they refused the delegation permission to cross their borders?

 
  
 

1. At the recent meeting of the EU-Turkey Association Council on 16 April, the European Union again clearly expressed its concern at the current lack of respect for human rights and fundamental freedoms in Turkey, including the cultural rights of all its citizens irrespective of origin. On this occasion, the ΕU made a point of raising the question of the status of Turkish citizens of Kurdish extraction.

2. Although it noted that recent legislative reforms in Turkey had brought about a degree of improvement, the ΕU made it perfectly clear to Turkey that it needs to make more progress in the field of human rights and democratic freedoms if it is to meet the political accession criteria laid down in Copenhagen. The ΕU stressed the paramount importance of practical application in this context. More importantly, the ΕU expressed its humanitarian concern about hunger strikers refusing food to the death in their fight for prison reforms and the tragic loss of human lives as a result.

3. The ΕU will continue to use the association agreement, accession partnership and reinforced political dialogue to put pressure on Turkey to make practical, tangible improvements to respect for human rights and democratic freedoms, in line with the basic priorities of the partnership.

 

QUESTIONS TO THE COMMISSION
Question no 41 by Ilda Figueiredo (H-0162/02)
 Subject: Women's rights in Turkey
 

After meetings with Turkish representatives, it would seem that recognition of the Community acquis is restricted to the economic sphere. What measures does the Commission intend to take to promote women’s rights and equality between the sexes in Turkey?

 
  
 

Gender equality is covered by the Copenhagen political criteria. The Commission monitors closely the situation of woman's rights in Turkey.

In its regular report of October 2001, the Commission noted the initiatives taken by Turkey to strengthen gender equality. Amongst these initiatives are the adoption of constitutional amendments introducing the principle of equality between spouses and the abolition of discrimination between parents concerning the transmission of Turkish citizenship.

The new civil code adopted at the end of 2001 introduces further changes aimed at strengthening gender equality.

In the context of financial assistance, the Commission is currently implementing a number of programmes with a view to promoting women’s rights and equality between the sexes. These programmes, totalling several million euros, cover a wide variety of areas such as education, health, advocacy and the economic and social spheres.

 

Question no 42 by Horst Schnellhardt (H-0182/02)
 Subject: Speech by Commissioner Verheugen: Turkey's accession by 2004
 

In a speech at the inaugural meeting of the EU-Turkey Foundation in October 2001 at Concert Noble in Brussels, Commissioner Verheugen is reported to have said that Turkey's accession to the EU was possible by 2004.

Mr Verheugen's grounds for saying so were, reportedly, (a) that the world had fundamentally changed since 11 September 2001, and (b) that the most important Christian churches were present in Turkey.

Is this what was actually said?

If so, is accession by 2004 realistic in view of the Copenhagen criteria? How is it possible that no date has been mentioned at other events where has been no Turkish involvement?

Is it correct that Turkey has speaking rights at 'European conferences'? If so, on what basis have those rights been granted?

 
  
 

The references of the Commissioner in charge of Enlargement of 17 October 2001 to which the Honourable Member refers are not accurate.

In his speech, the Commissioner focused on recent development in Union-Turkey relations. He mentioned that "Turkey's preparations for Union membership are now going forward on the same basis as with all other candidate countries. Remarkable progress has been made in this regard. The implementation of the pre-accession strategy is well underway."

As for the events of 11 September 2001, he mentioned them in the context of the fight against terrorism which has become "a priority for both the Union and Turkey."

The Commissioner did not refer to the "presence of important Christian churches in Turkey."

Turkey's progress towards accession is assessed by the Commission on a yearly basis in its Regular Report. In the October 2001 Regular Report, the Commission concluded that Turkey still does not meet the Copenhagen political criteria.

The opening of accession negotiations will be possible only when Turkey, as any other candidate country, will fully comply with the Copenhagen political criteria.

In his speech, the Commissioner did not mention any date concerning Turkey's Union accession.

As for the European Conference, all candidate countries are among participants. For this reason, Turkey participates on an equal footing with all other candidate countries to these conferences.

 

Question no 43 by Lone Dybkjær (H-0184/02)
 Subject: Specific action to encourage women to stand as candidates in the 2004 European elections in the applicant countries
 

It is hoped that the applicant countries of Central and Eastern Europe will take part in the next European elections. This will change the composition of the European Parliament. We already know that women's participation and representation in politics in a number of Central and East European countries is below the EU average. If no action is taken to enable and encourage women to stand as candidates, who, it is hoped, will then be elected, the proportion of women in the European Parliament will be lower than it is today.

Can and will the Commission set aside funding, in the context of financial measures concerning enlargement, to improve women's prospects of standing for election, and being elected, to the European Parliament?

 
  
 

The Commission shares the view of the Honourable Member as regards the participation of applicant countries in the next European elections. Following the Laeken conclusions, the Union is determined to bring the accession negotiations with the candidate countries that are ready to a successful conclusion by the end of 2002, so that those countries can take part in the Parliament elections in 2004 as members.

As regards gender equality, the Commission draws the attention of the Honourable Member to the fact that the accession criteria laid down by the European Council in Copenhagen for countries wishing to join the Union also include the acquis on non-discrimination and gender equality. The Commission is thoroughly monitoring the progress made by candidate countries in this respect. In its 2001 Strategy Paper it concluded that “further progress was made in several countries in ensuring gender equality, both in terms of legislation and the institutional framework. However, further efforts are needed to promote the economic and social equality of women as it is also the case in the Communauté.”

The Commission continues to support measures aiming at improving the gender equality in the context of its pre-accession strategy, e.g. through assistance in the framework of Phare National Programmes in various candidate countries following the priorities set out in the Accession Partnerships, or through the possible participation of candidate countries in the 2001-2005 Community Programme on Gender Equality, which covers also the promotion of equal participation and representation. It is however the responsibility of each country concerned to identify its particular needs for assistance under these programmes.

 

Question no 44 by Rosa Miguélez Ramos (H-0205/02)
 Subject: Moral harassment in the workplace
 

Twelve million workers, or 8% of the EU's workforce, claim that they have been victims of moral harassment in the workplace. Action to deal with this problem is made difficult by the lack of a definition and the resultant legal vacuum.

In its resolution of 20 September 2001 on harassment at the workplace, Parliament called for mental, psychosocial and social factors in the work environment to be taken into account with a view to strengthening the quality dimension in employment.

In view of this resolution, does the Commission intend to put forward initiatives to ensure that the EC legislates on the matter?

 
  
 

The Commission is fully aware of the serious implications of psychological harassment in the workplace, not only for the well-being and physical and emotional health of workers and of their families, but also for the organisational environment in companies and for efficiency and productivity in the workplace.

The creation of high-quality jobs implies not only stable employment, with fair economic, social and working conditions, but also protection of the health and safety of workers, which includes protection of their individual and collective dignity, particularly against possible harassment or humiliation in the workplace.

Risks of a psychosocial nature in the workplace, such as those involving harassment, must therefore be prevented and avoided.

The Commission has recently adopted its communication "Adapting to change in work and society: a new Community Strategy on health and safety at work 2002-2006", which adopts a global approach to well-being at work, taking account of changes in the world of work and the emergence of new risks, especially of a psychosocial nature. It is foreseen in the communication that the Commission will examine the appropriateness and the scope of a Community instrument on psychological harassment at work.

 

Question no 45 by Bernd Posselt (H-0167/02)
 Subject: Research funds and embryo protection
 

What is the Commission doing to prevent EU research funds being used for projects which are prohibited by the embryo protection legislation in force in many Member States?

 
 

Question no 46 by Brian Crowley (H-0173/02)
 Subject: 6th Framework Programme and adult stem-cell research
 

Does the Commission have any plans to draw up proposals under the 6th Framework Programme in favour of adult stem-cell research as opposed to embryonic stem-cell research?

 
 

Question no 47 by Dana Rosemary Scallon (H-0195/02)
 Subject: Research funds, embryo protection and Article 169
 

Does the Commission consider that Article 169 will apply to the funding of embryo research programmes?

In this context, if certain candidate countries decide to ban embryo research, this will exclude them from pubic funding under the 6th framework programme for research. How will the Commission ensure inclusiveness in EU public funding under the 6th framework programme for research without given an EU trend and pressurising countries to legalise embryo research, which is already illegal in four Member States?

 
 

Question no 48 by Lennart Sacrédeus (H-0198/02)
 Subject: EU funding of unlawful research
 

Four EU Member States - Germany, Austria, Ireland and Portugal - have passed laws prohibiting research which entails the destruction of embryos.

How does the Commission intend to ensure that account is taken of such national legislation when the European Union's 6th Framework Programme for research is drawn up? If joint EU funds are used to finance activities which are unlawful in these Member States, will this mean equal treatment or discrimination for them and their taxpayers, and does the Commission consider this to be a fair and proper principle that is applicable in the case of EU-funded projects?

 
 

Question no 50 by Bastiaan Belder (H-0247/02)
 Subject: Sixth Multiannual Framework Programme for research: embryo research
 

Does the Commission think it is fair that Member States are obliged to provide financial support for research activities that conflict with their national laws, which will be the case if the Framework Programme finances from Community resources research activities which conflict with the national laws of several Member States?

What can be done to prevent this situation leading to pressure on (future) Member States to liberalise their ethical and legal framework for research activities, in order to profit from European research funds? How does the Commission intend to prevent competition being affected by whether or not research on embryos is allowed? Is there any way, other than excluding these activities from funding under the Framework Programme, to prevent this undesirable situation and unfair competition, while taking full account of the subsidiarity principle?

Joint Answer

Research involving the use of adult stem cells seeking to develop new cell therapy techniques is funded under the 5th Framework Programme and will also be encouraged in the 6th Framework Programme.

In line with Parliament’s amendment at first reading to the 6th Framework Programme and in line with the opinion of the European Group on Ethics, the Commission considers that we should not at this stage, when knowledge is still limited, deny ourselves the option of exploring the opportunities presented by all the potential sources of stem cells in addition to human embryos explicitly produced for this purpose. This includes adult human stem cells but also those originating from the blood from the umbilical cord or from interrupted pregnancies as well as cells from supernumerary human embryos.

This research could therefore be funded, provided that it is scientifically relevant, justified and respects the ethical regulations and codes currently in force in the countries in which the research will be taking place. The Commission requests that the agreement of the local ethical committees or responsible authorities in the country where the research is to take place be obtained prior to the start of the research. In addition, an ethical review panel at Commission level will be instructed to ascertain whether the project partners fulfil all the requisite conditions.

In the fields in which the Community has official competence, and this is the case with research, national legislation should not affect a strictly Community level action which has been decided according to the institutional procedure with the involvement of the Council and Parliament.

Moreover, such a possibility would run counter to the obligation of respect for diversity laid down in Article 22 of the European Union Charter of Fundamental Rights and Article 6 of the Treaty on European Union.

For reasons linked, in particular, to competence in the matter and to respect for cultural diversity in Europe, it is now the responsibility of present and future Member States to establish regulations in this field. These regulations will be defined through the democratic process in each State.

At first glance, there is nothing to prevent Article 169 being applied in the field of research on the human embryo provided that the prevailing regulations and codes are respected. However, to date, the Commission has not received any request to this effect from the Member States. It does not, therefore, intend to make any proposal subject to the codecision procedures laid down for the implementation of Article 169 of the EC Treaty.

 
 

Question no 49 by Claude Moraes (H-0210/02)
 Subject: Age discrimination in the field of Research and Development
 

In response to my question P-1375/01(1), the Commission stated that the directing of certain training elements of the MFP 2002-2006 towards young researchers will not preclude participation by older researchers who have started their research careers later in life. In light of this, will the Commission state how it is still possible for vacancies for Research Training Networks to be posted exclusively for 'young researchers'?

Will the Commission consider that limiting applications to recently qualified scientists, rather than those under 35 years of age, would be a more appropriate method of helping researchers in the establishment of their careers?

Consecutive Council meetings restate the dual aim of increasing lifelong learning in the EU and encouraging older workers to stay in the workforce. Should the Commission not therefore recognise that their recruitment policies should reflect the EU's wider aims and objectives?

 
  
 

The honourable Member is right to point out that there should no longer be an age limit for the potential beneficiaries of Commission actions promoting the training and professional development of researchers. The Commission’s proposal for the new framework programme (2001-2006) no longer mentions an age limit. It proposes to distinguish between researchers who are in an initial phase of their career (whatever their age) and more experienced researchers, as the honourable Member recommends. This Commission position received the approval of Parliament and the Council at first reading under the codecision procedure.

The ‘Research Training Networks’ referred to specifically in the honourable Member’s question will serve this dual purpose in that they will have to be clearly based on a fair balance between the two ‘categories’ of researchers: in addition to providing training for researchers at the beginning of their career, they will be open to experienced researchers and thus be a useful knowledge-sharing mechanism.

However, it is impossible for the Commission to change the conditions for eligibility for the ‘Research Training Networks’ for the 5th Framework Programme currently in progress. These conditions derive from decisions relating to the 5th Framework Programme, which stipulate explicitly that the networks must be geared towards the training of ‘young’ researchers, and from the subsequent establishment of a standard contract setting the age limit at 35.

 
 

(1) OJ C 318 E, 13.11.2001, p. 238.

 

Question no 51 by Bart Staes (H-0159/02)
 Subject: Progress of the Commission's inquiries into unlawful State aid and violations of competition law
 

In answer to oral question H-0341/00(1), the Commission stated that it had begun to make informal inquiries into the State aid which De Post and the ABX parcel service might have received. The merger between the two did not go ahead at the time, but private courier services continue to claim that unlawful State aid is being provided, particularly to ABX, thus distorting competition.

More recently, the Commission stated in reply to question P-3477/01 that it had received information from the Belgian authorities ‘in order to be able to assess the nature of any advantages enjoyed by Ryanair in the light of the rules on State aid laid down in the EC Treaty’. Charleroi airport is said to be providing unlawful aid in order to retain the Irish airline’s flights.

What progress has been made in these two inquiries? What concrete facts can the Commission provide in order at long last to clarify these matters, which have remained unresolved for too long?

 
  
 

The Commission has recently been in communication with the Belgian authorities in order to assess, at their request, the impact of various SNCB projects to restructure ABX activity.

There have been allegations in the press that SNCB has made contributions which may amount to as much as EUR 250 million, whereas a decision of the Council of Ministers limits this recapitalisation to EUR 50 million.

In the case in point, the Belgian authorities were informed during this period of communication that the possibility of there being funds constituting State aid could not be excluded. A more detailed enquiry is being carried out with due regard for the confidentiality rules relating to this field.

With regard to State aid received by La Poste, information has indeed been exchanged with the Belgian authorities. This exchange related essentially to a project that has not been implemented.

The Commission has also just received a complaint regarding unfair competition from the Association belge des agences de publicite [Belgian association of advertising agencies]. The Commission has just started to investigate this complaint.

With regard to the agreements concluded with the Walloon Region, Charleroi airport and the airline, Ryanair, the Commission departments have completed their analysis of the information in their possession, specifically information provided by the Belgian authorities. However, the Commission departments do not have all the information necessary to be able to satisfy themselves that these agreements do not contain elements of State aid. The next stage in the investigation is therefore to send a request for supplementary information to the Belgian authorities.
