Question no 52 by Jillian Evans (H-0160/02)
 Subject: UK Government lobbying on behalf of business interests
 

On 23 July 2001 the UK Prime Minister sent a letter to the Romanian Prime Minister supporting the bid of the LNM Group to buy the formerly state-owned SIDEX steel plant in Romania. The LNM Group is owned by Lakshmi Mittal, who donated GBP 125 000 to Mr Blair’s party in June 2001. Prime Minister Blair stated that it would be 'an important step forward in the efforts you and your government are making to restructure and modernise your country’s economy' and would help Romania’s application for candidacy of the EU.

Is this kind of lobbying of the Romanian Prime Minister by the UK Prime Minister a breach of EU competition rules?

 
  
 

The Honourable Member had already written in this matter to the Commission and received an answer with letter of 5 March of this year. The Commission is not able to add any additional information to what was contained in this letter. The competition rules of the Treaty concern in the area of antitrust and mergers the behaviour of undertakings. In the area of State aid they concern financial support to undertakings by Member States. The Commission does therefore not see how the behaviour described by the Honourable Member could breach these competition rules.

Furthermore it is not for the Commission to comment on communications between Heads of Government of Member States and Governments of third countries.

 

Question no 53 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H-0194/02)
 Subject: Transfer of production from Gislaved
 

The German tyre-maker Continental has decided to cease manufacturing at Gislaved in Sweden, one of the reasons being that production is being transferred to an expanded plant at Lousado in Portugal. A number of reports have emerged indicating that the new plant in Portugal has received both EU subsidies and tax breaks from the Portuguese State. The Commission, through Commissioner Monti, has promised to investigate whether such aid is compatible with EU rules.

What are the conclusions of this investigation? When is the investigation expected to be complete?

 
 

Question no 55 by Hans Karlsson (H-0246/02)
 Subject: Tax subsidies
 

Continental has decided to stop its production of tyres at Gislaved in Sweden. Questions have already been tabled on this subject in conjunction with the fact that the company received structural aid to relocate from one Member State to another. It has also since emerged that the Portuguese government granted the company tax concessions to expand its operations in Portugal. In total, Continental has received more than EUR 6 million in tax concessions.

How is this situation consistent with the objective of competition neutrality between companies within the Union?

 
  
 

The Commission has requested the Portuguese government to provide the information necessary to enable it to assess presumed aid measures in the form of tax breaks to the tyre manufacturer Mabor-Continental in Lousado under the EC Treaty rules on state aid. This information is to be received soon.

If it appears that the alleged measures are covered by approved aid schemes and that the aid measures to the company were not notifiable to the Commission, the investigation can be closed within a few weeks. If the information shows, however, that the measures should have been notified to the Commission, an in depth investigation will be carried out. In this case the treatment and assessment of these measures are to require the period envisaged in the procedural guidelines on state aid. The period required to finalise the investigation may vary depending on the type of procedure that must be followed in the case and on the type of decision to be taken.

The Commission’s approach to regional aid is in the first place defined by setting aid ceilings for regions, taking into account the specific conditions of economic development that apply in the region concerned. Aid schemes can be approved when they apply in a general way to any company that wishes to invest in such a region. In other words, they should be neutral as concerns the sector in which the company is operating. A higher aid level can be allowed if the company is a small and medium-sized enterprise.

 

Question no 54 by Giles Bryan Chichester (H-0211/02)
 Subject: Europe Agreement with Romania and State Aids
 

Has the Commission received all necessary business plans or applications under Protocol II Article 9 of the Europe Agreement establishing an association between the European Economic Communities and their Member States, of the one part, and Romania, of the other part (21994A1231(20))?

 
  
 

The Commission has received the viability plans prepared by the Romanian Ministry of Industry relating to the State owned steel companies. The level of detail of those plans does not allow the Commission, however, to assess whether the conditions of Protocol 2 of the agreement are met for aid to be authorised. It has therefore requested the Romanian authorities to provide more detailed information on these plans as well as on the restructuring plans of the privatised companies that receive State aid for restructuring purposes.

The Commission remains in close contact with the Romanian authorities in order to ensure that the individual restructuring plans lead to the viability of the firms concerned, that the amount of the aid is proportional to the restructuring effort and that the plans are linked to a global rationalisation and reduction of capacity in Romania.

 

Question no 56 by Hans-Peter Martin (H-0254/02)
 Subject: Investigation of mergers
 

In 2000 under the responsibility of EU Commissioner Mario Monti far over 300 mergers were investigated. The year before even fewer than 300 were.

How many mergers were investigated in 2001? How does the Commission assess the quantity and quality of the work done under its responsibility in the area of merger control? What are the strengths and weaknesses, and what developments – in terms of both quantity and quality – are likely in the next few years?

 
  
 

In 2001, 335 planned company mergers were notified to the Commission in accordance with Regulation (EEC) No 4064/89 on the control of company mergers1. In 332 cases, the Commission took a decision in the light of the planned merger’s compatibility with the Common Market, and it was possible for the vast majority of cases to be concluded within the period of a month. In 20 cases (referred to as Phase II cases), more thorough investigations, leading to decisions within a total period of five months, were necessary because of serious reservations from a competition angle.

Because of the general economic situation and the uncertainty of the share markets, there has been a slight fall in the number of company mergers notified since the last quarter of 2001, and this has led overall to stabilisation at a relatively high level.

By means of a rapid procedure conducted within the statutory periods, the Commission examines every company merger in terms of its compatibility with the Common Market and makes a decision in every individual case, for which it gives its reasons. Any problems from a competition angle that may arise are thoroughly investigated. The investigation is carried out in a transparent manner. There is close contact between the Commission and the parties concerned, and the Member States are given the opportunity to adopt positions on the matter. The rights of the companies involved in the merger are secured by providing for consultation, the inspection of documentation and the formal transmission of specific complaints. Where it is applied, the procedure has the additional advantage for companies of providing what is termed a ‘one-stop shop’ through which multiple notifications to different national competition authorities in the Member States can be avoided.

Moreover, the Commission has endeavoured to go on improving the procedure. For example, the Green Paper of 11 December (1)2 concerning the revision of Council Regulation (EEC) No 4064/89 submits proposals for further intensifying cooperation with the parties by means, for example, of the possibility of introducing, upon request and for a limited period, an interruption of period for submitting binding promises.

Furthermore, the Commission will, in the future too, publish guidelines and announcements concerning its administrative practice in order to make it easier for companies to apply the regulations governing the control of mergers, to enhance legal certainty and to make the procedure still more transparent.

 
 

(1) OJ L 040 13.2.1998
2 COM (2001) 745 final

 

Question no 57 by Mihail Papayannakis (H-0165/02)
 Subject: Checks on pesticides
 

In its report of 27 February 2002, the General Consumer Federation of Greece (INKA) criticises the checks carried out in Greece on the use of pesticides during harvesting as piecemeal and inadequate. The report claims that the chronic lack of measures and checks has created a 'chemical bomb' which threatens the health of producers and consumers, the environment and ground water.

Since high levels of pesticides and chemicals have been found in fruit and vegetables intended for domestic consumption and for export to other Member States, in some cases as much as 25 times the permissible limits, what steps will the Commission take to ensure that the Greek authorities carry out effective checks in order to protect European consumers?

 
  
 

The Commission has taken the following steps to ensure that Member States carry out a minimum of check sampling of pesticide residues on their food market.

The annual Monitoring Recommendations provide for the monitoring of residues of about 44 pesticides on 12 food products. The minimum number of samples to be taken are 12 for Greece. The Recommendation is not binding, but so far Greece has always submitted such data to the Commission, which forms part of the Annual EU report on Pesticide Residues monitoring, published on the Internet.

In addition Greece has to provide the Commission with its annual monitoring plan in which Greece discloses its intentions for national monitoring of pesticide residues outside the scope of the Union co-ordinated plan. The data resulting from this programme must also be sent to the Commission and are included in the annual report.

The annual reports available from 1996 to 1999 do not suggest large scale violations of the Pesticide Residues legislation in Greece: 4% of all samples exceed the Maximum Residue Limit (MRL’s), which is around the same as the European average.

MRLs are set to protect the health of consumers, but are in most cases set much lower – in some cases more than 25 times lower - because the authorisation for the use of pesticides allows only the minimum amount needed for proper control of the pest. When residues are found in excess of the MRL and the levels pose a potential risk to the health of any group of consumers, this is immediately reported to the Commission and the Member States authorities through the Rapid Alert System.

 

Question no 58 by Caroline F. Jackson (H-0178/02)
 Subject: Travel guide swindles
 

It has come to light that a number of companies, based both within the EU and outside of it, are operating a probable swindle relating to travel guides. The victim (usually a commercial company) receives an initial appeal, seemingly just for information to go into a travel guide. Once this has been signed and returned however, the victim then receives a large bill for inclusion in the guide, followed by repeated demands for payment.

While individual consumers are protected against such schemes by EU legislation, it appears that commercial companies are not. Can the Commission state what action it is taking to close this loophole, and can it confirm whether any action is being taken against the offending companies?

 
  
 

The Commission shares the Honourable Member’s concerns about these marketing practices. The Commission has noted a significant rise in the number of complaints relating to scams involving the marketing of travel guides, particularly targeting small and medium sized enterprises.

These unfair commercial practices and the ‘rogue traders’ who employ them undermine competition and confidence in the internal market.

The Commission has analysed the complaints to determine whether Community laws have been infringed. These practices may be considered as “misleading" under the Council Directive on Misleading Advertisements which aims to protect consumers and business from the effects of misleading advertising. This directive has been transposed into all Member States national legal systems and the directive does foresee the use of injunctions as a preventive measure for stopping the continued use of misleading advertising.

The Commission has no power to enforce this directive against individuals, as this is the competence of national enforcement authorities in the countries from which these mailshots are sent. The Commission would be ready to start infringement proceedings if it became evident that Member States were not applying the Directive, for example by enforcing the foreseen sanctions.

Therefore the Commission has already alerted Member State enforcement authorities using the European group of the International Marketing Supervision Network (IMSN). Precise details of the companies involved in marketing these travel guides have been given to the enforcers. They can therefore consider what steps can be taken either individually or collectively.

Last year, for instance, the British office of Fair Trading and Spanish Catalan Authorities successfully co-operated to stop one firm, based in Spain, marketing a travel guide from continuing to use misleading mailshots. This kind of co-operation is to be strongly encouraged.

In addition, the Commission has also passed on this information to the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) supervisory authority in relation to travel guides which are being marketed by rogue traders operating out of the European Economic Area (EEA) countries, particularly Liechtenstein.

 

Question no 59 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H-0181/02)
 Subject: Plant health controls in the Canary Islands
 

Owing to mass imports of agricultural products under the open market arrangements imposed by the WTO and the European Union's new liberalisation policy, serious plant diseases have been introduced into the Canary Islands - an area which used to be covered by special protection laws. The diseases introduced into the islands include woolly white fly and tomato yellow leaf curl virus, the latter of which has devastated the islands' tomato production. What steps does the Commission intend to take to tighten up plant health controls and compensate the farmers affected?

 
  
 

The Union plant health regime is based on Council Directive 2000/29/EC1 on protective measures against the introduction into the Community of organisms harmful to plants or plant products and against their spread within the Community. This Directive lays down, amongst other things, the list of organisms the introduction or spread of which within the Community shall be banned. Both the white fly and the tomato yellow leaf curl virus are part of this list. As a consequence, the Member States have to take appropriate measures in order to prevent the introduction or spread of these two organisms.

However, specific phytosanitary provisions have to be approved in order to accommodate fully the climatic and agricultural specificity of the Canary Islands. Until then, Directive 2000/29/EC is not applicable to this part of the Spanish territory.

The finalisation of these specific phytosanitary provisions requires a substantial amount of technical data, some of them still to be submitted by Spain. The Commission will take immediate action once this information is made available.

Concerning possible compensation for damage caused by plagues, the Commission, as a general rule, does not accept that outbreaks of plant pests and diseases can be considered to constitute natural disaster or exceptional occurrence. In the case set out by the Honourable Member, the Commission cannot grant a type of compensation that might have indemnified the affected farmers, neither in the framework of rural development plans, nor within the Common Organisation of the Market for fruit and vegetables. However, some pest management costs may be covered by Producer Organisations operational programmes, in accordance with the provisions of Regulation (EC) No 609/2001.

In addition, under certain circumstances, and following notification to and approval by the Commission, Member States may provide State aid to producers hit by plant diseases. The criteria for this are set out in point 11.4 of the “Community guidelines for state aid in the agriculture sector”.

 

Question no 60 by William Francis Newton Dunn (H-0185/02)
 Subject: Tobacco advertising
 

How many of the 15 EU Member States have conformed with the directive on advertising of tobacco products?

 
  
 

At present, there is no Directive completely covering the advertising of tobacco products. In fact, the Directive on tobacco advertising and sponsorship adopted by the Parliament and the Council in 1998 was annulled by the Court of Justice (ECJ) in October 2000. This limits the Community “acquis” in this field to the prohibition of tobacco advertising on television, as a result of the “Television without Frontiers” Directive of 1989.

However, immediately after the Court’s judgement, the Commission prepared a new proposal for a Directive on tobacco advertising and sponsorship, which was adopted by the Commission in May 2001. This new proposal is fully consistent with the ECJ judgement; it foresees inter alia the prohibition of advertising in newspapers and magazines, on the Internet and on the radio, as well as a prohibition of the sponsoring of cross-border events.

The Commission would like to remind the Honourable Member of the fact that this proposal, which the Commission transmitted to Parliament in June 2001, has still not had a first reading in this Institution, a situation that seriously delays the adoption of this important piece of tobacco control legislation.

The Economic and Social Committee adopted a favourable opinion on the Commission’s proposal in October 2001. The Parliament’s Environment Committee approved their draft opinion in March this year, and together with the Legal Affairs Committee will conduct a hearing on the issue on 15 April this year. The Commissioner in charge of Health and Consumer Protection addressed the Legal Affairs Committee on this proposal on 26 March. The Council hopes to discuss this proposal at the Health Council on 26 June. The Commission notes that the first reading in Parliament is so far scheduled for July.

Although there is at present no legislation in this area, some Member States have in fact introduced legislation that corresponds to the provisions of the annulled advertising Directive, or even goes beyond it.

In Denmark, for example, such legislation entered into force on 1 January 2002. In the Netherlands, a Bill whose scope is very similar to that of the annulled Directive is in the last phase of parliamentary approval. In Ireland, a new Bill covering large parts of the annulled Directive has been enacted recently and will enter into force soon. In the United Kingdom, a Tobacco Advertising and Promotion Bill is before Parliament and the Government hopes it will be enacted this autumn. Moreover, Spain is preparing legislation closely based on the provisions of the annulled Directive.

Therefore, a number of Member States have introduced, or are in the process of preparing, far-reaching legislation in this area – motivated by concern at the link between tobacco promotion and tobacco consumption. On the contrary, the situation in other Member States is still characterised by the absence of stringent legislation on tobacco advertising. There is a resulting variation in national laws, and it has become clear that the divergences between these national laws may have the effect of strengthening barriers that impede the free circulation of advertising and sponsorship products and services between Member States.

These barriers need to be removed to assure the smooth operation of the single market, hence the justification for this proposal.

The Commission calls on the Parliament to speed up the work on this draft Directive.

 

Question no 61 by Marit Paulsen (H-0186/02)
 Subject: New rules on the control of feedingstuffs and foodstuffs
 

Again and again, we learn of new food safety crises in Europe. Just over a month ago, carcinogenic PCB residues were found in pig feed and poultry feed. And residues of allergenic antibiotics were recently discovered in feed from a Belgian factory.

It is also rumoured that the Commission's proposal for a regulation on the control of feedingstuffs and foodstuffs has been delayed because of differences of opinion between directorates.

In view of these recurrent serious problems, does the Commission not consider that this matter ought to be given top priority? When does it actually intend to present its proposal for new control rules? And will these control rules cover the (mis)use of antibiotics?

 
  
 

The Commission attaches a high priority to the preparation of a proposal on feed and food controls, a key priority in the White Paper on Food Safety. It must be pointed out however that the issues for this proposal are of a very complex nature with far reaching legal consequences. They need careful reflection.

In general, the proposal aims to have a clear Community framework for control systems, in which the respective responsibilities of Member States and the Commission are systematically set out.

Member States National Control Systems will have to be designed and developed in accordance with a harmonised Community-wide approach. This will involve establishing operational criteria for the national control authorities, qualification and training requirements for their staff, and the implementation of documented control procedures. This also includes the taking of effective, dissuasive and proportionate enforcement measures where non-compliance is identified.

It is in particular being considered within the Commission services to include in the proposal a section on administrative and penal sanctions to be imposed in case of breach of Community and national feed and food law. This includes the misuse of antibiotics. More precisely, the Commission is reflecting on possible penal sanctions if feed and food controls reveal the use of unauthorised or prohibited additives in feed or foodstuffs in breach of Directives 70/524/EEC (feed additives) and 95/2/EC (food additives) respectively.

The Commission’s responsibilities include the auditing of the control systems applied by the Member States and the taking of enforcement measures in case serious shortcomings are identified in these systems. One of the enforcement measures still under discussion within the Commission services is the possible withdrawal of Community funding granted under the Regulation on support for rural development from the European Agricultural Guidance and Guarantee Fund.

Further responsibilities of the Commission include the auditing of third countries so as to verify whether the conditions of production of feed and food destined for the Community market are of the same standard or of a standard equivalent to that applied by the Community.

The proposal will embrace all feed and food law, in a similar fashion to Regulation 178/2002 of the Parliament and of the Council laying down the general principles and requirements of food law, establishing the European Food Safety Authority and laying down procedures in matters of food safety.

It is the intention of the Commission to submit this proposal before August 2002.

 

Question no 62 by Peter William Skinner (H-0235/02)
 Subject: Health and safety provisions for food products
 

Recently, the port of Southampton has analysed various shipments of food from non-EU boats. The reports from scientists suggest significant problems and health risks with that food - particularly raw fish. Now that non-EU boats are aware of the potential of not getting their food past ports such as Southampton, they are moving on to other ports, where inspection and analysis are seemingly more lax. The loads are then re-imported into the UK and processed as EU safe food when this would have been forbidden entry into the UK before. The City of Southampton Council believes that it can prove this with several shipments it has tracked.

Does the Commission accept that ports interpret EU health and safety provisions for food products differently in different Member States? Given the way intra-trade arrangements within the EU can take place should not the Commission take action to secure the highest interpretative value of such imports so that food safety can be assured?

 
  
 

Council Directive 91/493/EC requires that the health conditions for fishery products imported from third countries must be at least equivalent to those applicable to these products originating from the Community.

The Commission is working towards further harmonising the microbiological criteria for the production of fishery products, on which the Scientific Committee is currently evaluating the available scientific information.

Checks on imports of products of animal origin are harmonised and clearly laid down in Council Directive 97/78/EC which specifies the documentary, identity and physical checks that must be carried out in any border inspection post in the Community1.

This legislation also lays down the rules which must apply to consignments which do not fulfil Community import requirements. In case of re-dispatch, the official veterinarian of the border inspection post must invalidate the import certificate and inform the other Community border inspection posts. If any of the checks indicates that a danger to human health exists, the consignment must be destroyed.

Details for the physical checks, including chemical and microbiological testing required for particular high-risk products, such as fish, subject to safeguard decisions, are normally specified in the relevant decision.

When there are no harmonised measures applicable to imports of products of animal origin, Member States are required to submit consignments to tests according to national rules.

Certain problems in the harmonisation of import checks for fishery products in the Member States have been identified during the last few years. The Commission is investigating with the Member States if harmonisation should be strengthened in particular as regards physical checks.

In the case of fresh fishery products directly landed on the territory of Member States by a fishing vessel flying a third country flag, Community legislation requires that these fishery products undergo the same veterinary checks as laid down in respect of fish landed by fishing vessels flying the flag of Member States.

Commission staff of the Food and Veterinary Office conduct regular inspections of all border inspection posts in the Member States to verify that correct Community standards of facilities, staffing and procedures are maintained. The reports of their inspections are made public, and are available on the Commission’s web-site.

Should any documented large discrepancies in application of Community legislation be brought to the attention of the Commission, this will be investigated and further action will be taken.

 

Question no 63 by Patricia McKenna (H-0260/02)
 Subject: Public exposure to electromagnetic fields
 

The Commission has just issued an implementation report on Council Recommendation 1999/519/EC(1) limiting public exposure to electromagnetic fields. The Council Recommendation of July 1999 asks Member States to adopt a protection framework based on the Recommendation into their national legislation and implement measures according to this framework.

Would the Commission consider introducing a common legislatory framework to compel Member States to comply with the best safety practices and remedy the disparity?

In the implementation report, Ireland states that it has submitted a revised Planning and Development Act in November 2001. However, Ireland fails to mention that this revision actually exempts companies erecting mobile phone masts from planning permission. This will in fact reduce the level of protection of the public and it loosens the regulatory framework for mobile phone masts. Is the Commission satisfied that it meets the demands set out above in the Council Recommendation?

 
  
 

The Honourable Member’s question refers to the Community framework established by the Council Recommendation of 12 July 1999 limiting the exposure of the general public to electromagnetic fields. The scope of this Recommendation covers the whole spectrum of non-ionising radiation, including emissions from devices such as mobile telephone systems. It calls on Member States to adopt the proposed set of limit values and to implement the measures necessary in order to limit exposure of the public to the levels given in the Recommendation.

The constant development in the use of electricity, and in particular the rapid development of mobile telecommunications systems, including the installation of transmitter masts, has given rise to some public concern. The Commission is very much aware of this, and the Recommendation just referred to addresses these concerns in a very comprehensive way, based on the opinion of leading experts and relevant European committees. However, as a Council Recommendation is not a binding legal instrument, Member States may adopt higher or lower levels of protection. Given the limitations of the EC Treaty, and in particular of Article 152 on public health which explicitly excludes, with very few exceptions, any harmonisation of the laws and regulations of Member States, the Community cannot adopt legislation in this area based on public health grounds.

However, at European level there are other binding instruments which effectively limit total body exposure to non-ionising radiation, and which compel Member States to comply with the best safety practices. Firstly, the Council Recommendation invites the Commission to work towards the establishment of European standards of compliance with the recommended basic restrictions. This is currently done within the framework of the mandate M/305 given to Cenelec, the European standardisation organisation for electrical equipment. This mandate derives directly from the Directives on low voltage and radio terminal and telecommunications equipment. On this basis Cenelec will develop standards which will replace conflicting national standards and ensure that eventually all devices emitting non-ionising radiation put on the market will meet the limits set up by the Council Recommendation. The first standard on mobile telephones was published in June 2001, and the standard on base stations is expected by July 2002.

Secondly, Union legislation governing radio products, including mobile telephone masts, obliges manufacturers to ensure that they are safe. Directive 1999/5/EC on Radio Equipment and Telecommunications Terminal Equipment obliges manufacturers to ensure that their products do not have an adverse effect on health and enables national authorities to impose restrictions on the use of radio products, e.g. by prescribing minimum distances from the public, so as to ensure that telephone masts are appropriately installed.

Therefore, beyond the provisions of the Council Recommendation, European standards and Community legislative provisions ensure that existing disparities will be remedied.

In general, the Irish implementation report demonstrates the willingness to take into account the limits recommended at Community level. The present lack of specific measures regarding planning permission should not be seen as an obstacle to the achievement of the Council Recommendation’s objectives. In fact, total body exposure will depend on the local power of each base station.

From a Commission perspective, therefore, we are not primarily concerned with Member States’ systems for granting planning permissions. The Commission is concerned about ensuring that the limit values which are set in the Council Recommendation, and which are reflected in the Cenelec standards, are respected. When this is the case the Honourable Member’s concerns regarding the level of public protection and maintaining an appropriate regulatory framework are effectively addressed.

The future European standard for base stations will define the conditions for putting antennas into service, taking into account other existing installations and their emissions. It will thereby ensure that in all cases, the recommended limits are not exceeded.

 
 

(1) OJ L 199 30.7.1999, p. 59.

 

Question no 64 by Eurig Wyn (H-0161/02)
 Subject: UK Government lobbying on behalf of business interests
 

On 23 July 2001 the UK Prime Minister sent a letter to the Romanian Prime Minister supporting the bid of the LNM Group to buy the formerly state-owned SIDEX steel plant in Romania. The LNM Group is owned by Lakshmi Mittal, who donated GBP 125 000 to Mr Blair’s party in June 2001. Prime Minister Blair stated that it would be 'an important step forward in the efforts you and your government are making to restructure and modernise your country’s economy' and would help Romania’s application for candidacy of the EU.

Will the Commission undertake a detailed inquiry into this lobbying on behalf of business interests?

 
  
 

Romania needs to accelerate the privatisation of state-owned companies if it is to be considered as a functioning market economy - and by doing so meet the Copenhagen economic criteria for enlargement. This point has been consistently made by the Commission in its Regular Reports on Romania’s Progress Towards Accession.

Against this context, the Commission and the major international financial institutions (the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund) welcomed the privatisation of SIDEX as a positive development. SIDEX is Romania’s largest single enterprise and its privatisation represents an important advance in modernising the Romanian economy.

The Commission has no evidence of any improprieties in the privatisation process. Furthermore, letters of support to individual companies are a matter for national governments and are not an area of Union competence.

The Commission has no intention to hold an inquiry on this subject.

 

Question no 65 by Sylviane H. Ainardi (H-0163/02)
 Subject: Women's associations in Turkey
 

Is the Commission aware of the situation regarding women’s organisations and associations in Turkey? Almost every year these organisations have to change their names and reorganise themselves because of interventions by the Turkish authorities. What does the Commission intend to do to improve the situation?

 
  
 

The Commission considers that Women’s’ Associations are fully covered by the principle of freedom of association, a core element of the Copenhagen political criteria.

As noted in its Regular Report of October 2001, various restrictions to the exercise of fundamental freedoms still exist in Turkey. This includes the principle of freedom of association.

Besides monitoring closely the situation with respect to freedom of association in relation to women’s associations and gender equality, the Commission provides substantial assistance to Turkish women.

Of particular significance is a project managed by the Women’s association named KADER. The purpose of this project worth EUR 339 000 is to contribute to the democratisation process via the promotion of a greater role for women in political life and debate.

 

Question no 66 by Marianne Eriksson (H-0164/02)
 Subject: EU representation in Turkey
 

The EU Representation to Turkey has just been assigned 20 more officials. Does the Commission intend to designate some of them to deal with the issue of women’s rights in Turkey?

 
  
 

All sections of the Commission's Representation in Turkey carry out activities which, in one way or another, deal with women’s issue:

the Political Section of the Representation deals with women’s rights with regards to the political criteria of Copenhagen;

the Financial Co-operation, Co-ordination and Investment Section of the Representation covers the area of employment and social policy, and inter alia women’s and gender issues;

the Financial Co-operation, Institution Building & Civil Society Section deals with the monitoring and management of projects including projects on women’s rights. The Representation has been supporting non-governmental organisations' (NGO) projects since 1993 throughout Turkey. A special emphasis has been given to the funding of women’s NGOs and projects dealing with women’s rights and issues. Further projects on women’s social, economic, legal and political rights are under development.

 

Question no 67 by Marialiese Flemming (H-0168/02)
 Subject: Temelin nuclear power plant
 

Following the elections in the Czech Republic, is the Commission prepared to initiate talks with a new government in Prague concerning a closure deadline for the South Bohemian Temelin nuclear power plant, where a total of 30 incidents have occurred during trial operation?

Is the Commission prepared to offer financial assistance to a new Czech government in fresh negotiations on a deadline for closing down the Temelin nuclear power plant?

 
  
 

The Commission recalls that the Union has based its position with regard to nuclear safety in all candidate countries on the Council Report on Nuclear Safety in the Context of Enlargement of June 2001. This report concludes that the “safety improvement programme for the Temelin units has been the most comprehensive so far for this reactor type. Completion of these programmes at […] Temelin is essential to remove the few remaining deviations at these plants with regard to achieving a high level of nuclear safety.”

Given this technical evaluation as well as the demands and recommendations that the Council submitted to the Czech authorities, the Commission sees no justification to support calls for an early closure of the Temelin Nuclear Power Plant (NPP). The purpose of the current trial phase is to identify and remedy remaining potential weaknesses. All occurring anomalies or incidents during this phase, as have been widely reported in some media, have been classified as “zero” or “category one” events on the International Nuclear Event Scale (INES), meaning without radioprotection concern. The Czech authorities regularly identify their root causes. These events have not changed the overall European Union expert evaluation of the Temelin NPP mentioned above.

The responsibility to maintain a high level of nuclear safety at the plant is incumbent on the operator and the independent Czech nuclear regulatory authority. Following the debate last year in the Parliament on the idea of an international donor conference, neither the Czech Government, nor Member States have signalled any interest in the provision of financial assistance related to an early closure of the Temelin plant. In any case there are no compelling technical reasons for such a closure. Therefore, the Commission sees no need for committing Community funds to this purpose.

 

Question no 68 by Alexandros Alavanos (H-0169/02)
 Subject: Unfair terms of contracts
 

In order to obtain the Community aid paid for their products, Greek farmers are 'required' to sign two standing orders and authorisations, one in respect of the Agricultural Bank, through which the aid must be paid to farmers, and the second in respect of the farmers' cooperative. The 'standing orders', which are for an indefinite period, authorise the bank and the cooperative to debit from the account in which farmers' aid is deposited any payments due to (1) the inland revenue in respect of tax or other dues, (2) ELGA (the farmers' insurance organisation), and (3) any other body. The positions from which the two parties to the agreement negotiate are unequal since the producer/consumer is compelled to use the services of the Agricultural Bank in order to receive the aid to which he is entitled and is persuaded to sign the standing orders both in respect of the bank and his cooperative, otherwise payment of the aid will be delayed.

Does the Commission, therefore, not consider that such a vaguely formulated, 'carte blanche' standing order authorising the payment of any form of sum due to third parties falls within the scope of Directive 93/13/EEC(1) on unfair terms in consumer contracts?

 
  
 

The Commission considers that Council Directive 93/13/EC, referred to by Mr Alavanos, on unfair terms in consumer contracts, is not applicable in this instance. Pursuant to Article 2(b) of this directive, consumer ‘means any natural person who, in contracts covered by this directive, is acting for purposes which are outside his trade, business or profession’. Now, clearly, the contractual relations established between the farmer, on the one hand, and the Greek Agricultural Bank and the farmers’ cooperative to which the farmer belongs, on the other, relate to the procedures for the collection of taxes on Community subsidies and are therefore closely linked to the farmer’s professional activity.

For further information, the Commission refers Mr Alavanos to the answers it gave to written questions Nos E-128/02 and E-238/02, on the general issue of the taxation of Community subsidies to which Greek farmers are entitled.

 
 

(1) OJ L 95, 21.4.1993, p. 29

 

Question no 69 by Camilo Nogueira Román (H-0171/02)
 Subject: Free competition in the fishing industry
 

Has the Commission analysed the Green Paper on the future of the fishing industry from the point of view of its compatibility with the principle of free competition in the European Union, particularly as regards the continuation of the principle of relative stability which places certain Member States in a privileged position?

 
  
 

The Commission considers that the principle of relative stability should be maintained for the foreseeable future, in view of the continuing dependence of certain regions on fisheries, the major disruption that would be caused by abandoning the present system of national allocations to fishing rights and the absence of similar conditions of competition in the Member States, due to different attitudes towards public aid for the fisheries sector. The Community should, however, aim progressively to introduce more normal economic conditions, such as free competition between producers, freedom of investment and the elimination of public aid to the fisheries sector. Progress towards this goal should determine any future decision to eliminate “relative stability”.

 

Question no 70 by Anders Wijkman (H-0175/02)
 Subject: Capacity-building for information and communications technologies and sustainable energy in EU development cooperation
 

Budget line B7-623 'Capacity-building for information and communication technologies and sustainable energy' was introduced as a new line in Category 4 of the budget for 2002. According to the remarks, the 'appropriation is intended for the Commission to establish a programme within its services to support capacity-building in developing countries for ICT and sustainable energy'. Furthermore, 'the program should be well co-ordinated with other donor initiatives on ICT and sustainable energy'. In light of this, and the crucial importance of these issues in relation to EU development cooperation efforts, and given that the first quarter of 2002 will soon come to an end, what developments and actions have there been in the Commission concerning this budget line to date? How does the Commission plan to ensure the full and effective use and implementation of this budget line?

 
  
 

In its recent communication to Parliament and the Council on this subject, the Commission highlighted the role that must be played by the information society and new technologies in development policy.

The proposed approach seeks to ensure that, where necessary, this issue will be taken into account horizontally in the sectoral programmes in the priority areas of our policy (health, education, economic cooperation etc.).

As regards the new budget line B7-623, the programming currently being carried out by the Commission departments provides for joint financing with multilateral institutions of capacity building programmes and, in particular, of development programmes already in progress which require additional financial resources. Initiatives already in progress such as those of the International Labour Organisation (ILO) (for new technologies and the information society) and the World Bank (for the development of capacities in the field of energy) are being analysed. A thematic network within the Commission departments has been set up with a view to taking advantage of internal expertise and planning procedures for action in this field and for implementing this budget line.

 

Question no 71 by Pedro Aparicio Sánchez (H-0176/02)
 Subject: Bicentenary of the birth of Victor Hugo
 

This year marks the two hundredth anniversary of the birth of Victor Hugo and France has organised a number of important events to commemorate the occasion.

This immortal writer’s visionary ideas concerning a united Europe, his dream of a future ‘United States of Europe’ and the international dimension of his work in the context of European Romanticism make him a symbol of European culture and a true precursor of the concept of a unified Europe.

In view of all this, does the Commission intend to organise events on a European scale to commemorate the bicentenary of Victor Hugo’s birth?

 
  
 

The Commission is aware of the importance of the European genius that is Victor Hugo. It is delighted to see that France is organising events to celebrate this European visionary.

Within the framework of the ‘Culture 2000’ programme (call for proposals 2002), it would have been perfectly possible for cultural operators to submit a project to celebrate the bicentenary of Victor Hugo. Regretfully, the Commission notes that no application for funding for such a project was submitted to commemorate this bicentenary.

Moreover, the ‘Culture 2000’ programme also provides, under action 3, for the funding of special cultural events with a European or international dimension such as the organisation of memorable innovative cultural events which are accessible to the citizens in general, particularly in the field of the cultural heritage, arts and history of Europe, also involving education, the arts and culture. It is within this framework that a year of Verdi was celebrated in 2001 and a year of Bach was celebrated as part of the preparations for the ‘Culture 2000’ programme in 1999.

Unfortunately, it has proved difficult to plan and make effective use of this opportunity, and it was not taken up by the Member States. However, with the backing of the parliamentary resolution adopted on the basis of the Ruffolo report, the Commission is seeking to develop this kind of action.

 

Question no 72 by Ioannis Koukiadis (H-0177/02)
 Subject: European aid to the Savisky Museum, Nukus (Karakalpakstan, Uzbekistan)
 

The Savisky Museum of Modern Art in Nukus (Karakalpakstan, Uzbekistan), which I visited during a mission by the delegation from the EU-Uzbekistan Parliamentary Cooperation Committee in the summer of 2001, contains genuine treasures of modern art. However, it is in an extremely precarious condition because of a lack of resources.

The building in which the museum is situated is in a pitiful state. The lighting which could be used to show these masterpieces to their best advantage actually consists in a few neon strips on the ceiling. All the walls are covered, from top to bottom, with paintings; other paintings are piled up in storage areas. And yet this collection has been compared to that of the Hermitage Museum, in St Petersburg.

Will the Commission state what programmes could provide assistance to this museum?

 
  
 

The Commission is aware of the difficulties of the Savisky Museum in Nukus in Uzbekistan. Aid measures have already been undertaken by the Member States which directly assist the museum and organise exhibitions of its works in Europe. A new building is in the process of being completed.

The priorities set for Tacis aid to Uzbekistan are (1) aid for institutional, legal and administrative reform, (2) aid intended alleviate the social consequences of the transition and (3) rural development. These priorities do not allow for funding of the operation to renovate the Savisky Museum.

The Commission is already assisting the authorities of the Autonomous Republic of Karakalpakstan, the capital of which is Nukus. A food security programme was launched as an extraordinary measure by the Commission in December 2001 to combat the effects of the drought suffered by this region bordering on the Aral Sea.

 

Question no 73 by Mary Elizabeth Banotti (H-0180/02)
 Subject: Professional activities of former senior civil servants following retirement from the EU institutions
 

Can the Commission confirm what the rules are governing the activities of senior retired officials serving on boards or with organisations where serious conflict of interest could potentially arise and is the Commission currently aware that a former senior official from DG Environment is now a member of the Board of British Nuclear Fuels?

 
  
 

Former permanent officials and other servants who have left the service are required to comply with Articles 16 and 17 of the Staff Regulations.

Article 16 in particular provides that, after termination of service with the Institution, the official must respect obligations of integrity and discretion in relation to the acceptance of certain duties or functions.

Article 17 relates to the necessary discretion with regard to the information acquired during the course of the official’s (former) duties.

In the specific case referred to by the Honourable Member, the Commission can confirm that it is in the process of examining whether the official in question will be in a position to comply with the relevant provisions of the Staff Regulations (Articles 16 and 17), in joining the board of directors of British Nuclear Fuels UK as an independent non-executive member after leaving the post of Director-General of DG Environment at the Commission. This examination should be finalised in the course of April.

In considering such cases the Commission expects its officials to refrain from accepting employment if this could lead to a conflict of interest with their former post in the Commission. Such a conflict is generally considered to be evident if the new activity is related to a dossier for which the official was responsible in the Commission and involves him/her in intervening against the position of the Commission with regard to this dossier. The same situation occurs where the official might profit from confidential information gained during his/her previous functions. Where the Commission suspects a violation of its professional rules it acts immediately to check the matter and, if necessary, takes measures to penalise such a violation.

In its proposal for modification of the Staff Regulations in the context of Administrative Reform, the Commission proposes to further strengthen Article 16 by placing an obligation on a former official to notify the Institution if he or she proposes to take up a professional activity within two years of termination of service. Similarly, the Commission proposes that Article 17 be reinforced in relation to divulgation of information not yet in the public domain.

 

Question no 74 by Glyn Ford (H-0189/02)
 Subject: North East Asian Development Bank
 

Does the Commission recognise the importance of infrastructure investment in North East Asia? Does it accept that the cheapest and most efficient way of achieving this may be through the establishment of a North East Asian Development Bank? If such a bank was to be established would the Commission support the EU becoming a shareholder?

 
  
 

The Commission recognises that infrastructure investment is key for future viable economic and commercial development in North East Asia, a region that covers a vast land area with big differences in factor endowments. Improvements in sectors such as transportation, energy, telecommunication and finance are seen as crucial in the current stage of economic co-operation and integration. Successful development in these fields will require abundant private investment. According to recent studies, the cost of upgrading and expanding the region’s infrastructure to international standards will amount to USD 7.5 billion annually for the next 15 to 20 years. However, although North East Asia undoubtedly has an enormous economic potential, it is still a very heterogeneous region, and national, political, institutional and bureaucratic impediments remain very high.

Financing for infrastructure investments in the region now come from three sources: the private sector, bilateral assistance, and International Financial Institutions (IFIs), in particular the World Bank (WB) and the Asian Development Bank (ADB).

At the current stage, both the ADB and the WB are already giving priority to regional, sub-regional, and national projects and programmes that will contribute most effectively to regional developments in various parts of Asia and the former Soviet Union (i.e. Central Asia, South Asia, the Mekong area). A similar focus could be envisaged towards North East Asia by the same IFIs. It seems therefore more appropriate, at this stage, to enhance co-ordination among existing IFIs rather than creating a new development bank in the region.

Member States are shareholders of the ADB and WB and the question of the possible Community representation in the governing bodies of such institutions is a matter that is not specifically related to the establishment of a North-East Asian Development Bank.

 

Question no 75 by Esko Olavi Seppänen (H-0191/02)
 Subject: National experts at the Commission
 

More than 800 officials are working at the Commission whose salaries are paid by the Member States. What costs does the Commission incur on their account and what is done to ensure that in their work they represent the EU rather than the Member State which pays them?

 
  
 

A seconded national expert (DNE) is entitled, throughout the period of secondment, to a daily subsistence allowance of EUR 104.03.

The allowance is payable for periods of missions, annual leave, special leave and holiday granted by the Commission.

Moreover, if the national expert has not removed his or her personal effects to the place of secondment, he or she is entitled each month to reimbursement of the cost of a return journey from his or her place of secondment to his or her place of recruitment provided that the place of recruitment is more than 100 Kilometres (km) from the place of secondment.

If the national expert has removed his or her personal effects, he or she is entitled each year for him/herself, his/her spouse and his/her dependant children to a flat-rate payment equal to the cost of a return journey from his/her place of secondment to his/her place of recruitment.

The national expert is also entitled to reimbursement of travel expenses for him/herself at the beginning and at the end of the secondment.

Travel expenses may also be reimbursed to his/her spouse and dependent children when removal takes place, at the beginning and at the end of the secondment.

The national expert is entitled to reimbursement of the costs of removing his/her personal effects provided that the period of secondment is at least two years and that the place of recruitment is more than 100 km from the place of secondment.

Seconded national experts are excluded from any official act in which the Commission exercises its public law prerogatives and they cannot enter into a commitment on the Commission’s behalf. However, national experts may work in any field where their services are deemed necessary provided there is no conflict with the interests of the Community. For this purpose, both the Head of the Unit to which it is intended to assign the national expert, and the national expert must declare in writing that there is no risk of a conflict of interests between the tasks the national expert performs for his/her employer or the professional activities of his/her close family, and the tasks entrusted to him or her as a seconded national expert. The national expert shall inform his or her hierarchical superior immediately of any change in this respect during the secondment.

In the context of the Commission’s proposals on Administrative Reform, and notably the use of non-permanent human resources, the Commission has approved proposals for a modification of the rules that are applicable to national experts with the purpose of reinforcing the principle that, in their work, national experts represent the Commission. The Commission Decision on National Experts will include explicit provisions to ensure that they are under an obligation to respect the same professional standards as officials. Furthermore, national experts should work on the basis of an exhaustive job description, under the double constraint of work under the guidance of permanent officials, and strict measures addressing the risk of conflict of interests. The formal Commission Decision on the revised "Rules applicable to National Experts on Detachment to the Commission” will be adopted before end of May 2002.

 

Question no 76 by Myrsini Zorba (H-0200/02)
 Subject: Diversity in publishing
 

Following successive takeovers and mergers in recent years, the publishing industry is suffering from numerous structural problems which are a threat to many small and medium-sized businesses. Publishing, however, is the main field for the dissemination of ideas and requires special measures to safeguard its diversity. The European book market encompasses an enormously broad range of views and ideas, which should be supported and maintained.

Will the Commission take measures to maintain cultural diversity in publishing and to protect small and medium-sized businesses in the industry?

 
  
 

The Commission is fully aware of the issues raised by the honourable Member. The implementation of Article 151 of the Treaty establishing the European Community requires that cultural diversity be respected. The Commission is already taking action along these lines, in particular by taking the cultural dimension into account in the other Community policies provided for in Article 151(4) of the Treaty establishing the European Community, with particular reference to competition policy.

Moreover, the Commission has taken note of the concerns expressed in Parliament and at the November 2001 Forum on Cultural Cooperation devoted to the needs of Europe’s culture industry.

 

Question no 77 by Chris Davies (H-0201/02)
 Subject: Rail freight through the Channel Tunnel
 

What action does the Commission propose to take to promote the use of rail through the Channel Tunnel to carry freight between the United Kingdom and continental Europe?

 
  
 

With reference to the objectives of the European transport policy, the Commission can only deplore the effects on rail freight traffic of the security problems at SNCF’s Fréthun terminal. The operations of this terminal, where trains are prepared for their journey through the Channel Tunnel, are in fact subject to constant disruption, and are even suspended, because of repeated attempts by illegally-resident immigrants to board these trains, come what may, in order to reach the United Kingdom and request asylum there.

This distressing matter, referred to at the Transport Council on 26 March, brings various Community policies into play.

One is that of ensuring the free movement of goods via a trans-European transport corridor, which means that sufficient, necessary and proportionate steps must be taken by France in order to ensure the passage of trains under the same non-discriminatory conditions. What is at issue is a question of fact. Since this situation arose in November last year, the Commission, applying Council Regulation (EC) 2679/98(1) of 7 December 1998 on the functioning of the internal market in relation to the free movement of goods among the Member States, has regularly asked France for all necessary explanations on the steps taken and on how and when they would be implemented. The Commission would be able to take action by default if it were not satisfied by these explanations. However, compensation for the parties sustaining loss due to the disruption of operations is a matter for the French courts.

On the other hand, one of the fundamental causes of the problem is that the Community framework for the free movement of persons has not yet been established as regards the crossing of borders and the exercise of the right of asylum. The Commission has drawn up all the proposals required to create this Community framework in matters of immigration and asylum, which are currently being discussed in the Council, where they are making slow progress.

In conclusion, the Commission appeals to the two Member States involved to implement in the short term the most appropriate practical measures to mitigate the consequences of the immediate problems, and, in particular, to make secure the terminals near the French end of the tunnel as well as, in the medium term, to promote the adoption of Community measures recently proposed by the Commission on immigration and the right of asylum, which are likely to finally eliminate the problem.

 
 

(1) OJ L 337/8 of 12 December 1998

 

Question no 78 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H-0203/02)
 Subject: The death sentence imposed on Safiya Hussaini Tungar-Tudu on grounds of adultery
 

What information can the Commission provide on the case of Safiya Hussaini Tungar-Tudu, who has been condemned to death by stoning in Nigeria?

 
  
 

Safiya Hussaini was sentenced to death by stoning by the Sokoto State Sharia court in Nigeria on 9th Oct 2001 for alleged adultery. The defendant appealed against the sentence and the hearing took place at the Sokoto Sharia Court of Appeal on 25th March 2002. The appeal was successful, primarily on the grounds that adultery took place prior to the introduction of Sharia law.

Shortly before the appeal the Attorney General of the Federation and Minister of Justice told the Governors of the 12 States operating the Sharia legal system to modify all criminal laws to conform with the constitution. This demand has been contested by some States as unconstitutional.

The Commission has followed this case closely and has been particularly concerned by both the human rights aspect of this case, as well as the use of the death penalty in general. The Commission has raised its concern about this case in a meeting with the Nigerian Ambassador in Brussels in December, and President Prodi in January appealed directly to President Obasanjo to intervene in this case.

 

Question no 79 by Proinsias De Rossa (H-0209/02)
 Subject: Sellafield MOX Plant
 

With regard to the decision by the UK Government to commission a MOX plant at Sellafield, and given that the Commission is presently examining the compliance of the procedure for granting the authorisation with all provisions of the Basic Safety Standards Directive (96/29/EURATOM)(1) and with the Directive on the assessment of the environmental impact (85/337/EEC)(2), will the Commission inform the European Parliament whether it will make public its findings and if so, when?

 
  
 

The British Government has recently replied to the request for information from the Commission concerning the procedure for granting the authorisation of the Sellafield MOX Plant (SMP).

With respect to the licensing procedure under the Basic Safety Standards Directive (96/29 EURATOM(3)), the analysis of the explanations given by the British authorities led to the conclusion that the necessary procedures were properly applied.

As regards the implementation of the Directive of 27 June 1985 on the assessment of the effects of certain public and private projects on the environment (85/337/EEC(4)), an extensive reply from the United Kingdom is still under preparation and should be available soon. The Parliament will be informed about the Commission conclusions with respect to this issue.
