Question no 80 by Niall Andrews (H-0214/02)
 Subject: Eliminating bottlenecks around conurbations
 

At the recent conference in Brussels on European Transport Policy for 2010, the need to eliminate bottlenecks around conurbations was emphasised in the context of having high Community added value for the Trans European Infrastructure Networks. Will the Commission make a statement on the issue and how it considers that a city such as Dublin could benefit from EU initiatives in this area?

 
  
 

In 1996 the Parliament and the Council adopted Decision 1692/96/EC of 23 July 1996 on Community guidelines for the development of the Trans-European Transport Network7. This decision established the guidelines covering the objectives, priorities and broad lines of measures envisaged in the area of the trans-European transport networks.

Article 9 of this Decision describes the criteria applying to the Trans-European road Network. This Article provides that the network shall comprise motorways and high-quality roads which, among other aspects, bypass the main urban centres on the routes identified by the network.

The network schemes are illustrated by maps attached to Decision 1692/96/EC. On map 2.7 it is indicated that the Dublin C-ring motorway is part of the Trans-European Networks outline plan for roads. This ringroad could be seen as a concrete example to eliminate bottlenecks around conurbations.

The recently adopted Commission proposal to update the guidelines does not alter the trans-European character of this route which would therefore remain eligible for Trans-European Network (TEN) Community funding.

Moreover under the Structural and Cohesion Funds, substantial support continues to be provided to tackle problems of congestion. In the case of congested roads in the Dublin area, the Cohesion Fund has financed the major part of the M50 C-ring motorway around the city. In regard to public transport, the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) also support the extension and improvement of suburban rail infrastructure and rolling stock, including light rail.

At the conference on the European transport policy for 2010, organised in Brussels on 27 November 2001, the third round table discussion was focussed on the future priorities of the trans-European transport network policy. The need to eliminate bottlenecks around conurbations was emphasised by some participants arguing that local or regional networks may determine the performance of inter-regional links and these networks should therefore not be excluded from the Trans-European Network. Other participants argued that these networks were nonetheless the responsibility of the regional authorities and this limits the scope for action by the Community. In the framework of the 2004 revision of the TEN-Transport Guidelines the Commission considers to elaborate in more detail on this bottleneck issue.

 

Question no 81 by Gerard Collins (H-0216/02)
 Subject: Agriculture and imports of 'Hilton beef' into EU
 

According to the Commission's own figures, the current allocation of the 'Hilton Beef' tariff quota for the USA and Canada, Australia, Uruguay, Brazil, New Zealand and Argentina amounts to 58 100 tonnes at a 20% ad valorem duty. Can the Commission indicate what the impact of these imports is on EU beef sales and whether it intends to permit additional imports into the EU of 'Hilton Beef' at these preferential rates of duty?

 
  
 

Although the total quantity of high quality beef under the World Trade Organisation (WTO) agreement amounts to 58 100 tonnes, only a part of that quantity is effectively imported. In the quota year 2000/2001 approximately 39 700 tonnes were imported under the preferential terms applicable to high quality beef. The imported quantity corresponded to just a little more than half a percentage point of the Community consumption of beef in that same period.

The Council is currently discussing a Commission proposal to open an autonomous quota of 1 000 tonnes of high quality beef at a duty-rate of 20% ad valorem. Furthermore, it is the intention of the Member of the Commission in charge of Agriculture and Fisheries to propose to the Commission and to the Council a supplementary quantity to the Community, in 2002/2003 only, of 10 000 tonnes also at a duty-rate of 20% ad valorem. It is expected that due to the return to normal market conditions with increased consumption levels, the possible additional high quality quota in 2002/2003 may be sold on the Community market without disturbing the market equilibrium.

 

Question no 82 by James (Jim) Fitzsimons (H-0218/02)
 Subject: Findings of Euratom Safeguards Office and Sellafield
 

The task of the Euratom Safeguards Office is to ensure that within the European Union nuclear material is not diverted from its intended use. The report of the operation of the Euratom Safeguards Office for the period 1999 to 2000 which was published in July 2001 points out that small discrepancies were still being investigated with the operators concerned. It points out furthermore that nuclear material still remains in the MOX-Demonstration Facility at Sellafield which has now been closed down. It also says that an important verification problem appears in some installations, including Sellafield where old plutonium-holding materials are stored. Difficulties of access and handling due to radiation protection and safety reasons do not permit full routine safeguards activities of these installations to be performed.

Is the Commission concerned about these findings and will it now indicate what the up-to date position is in relation to the discrepancies under investigation and where they were uncovered and will it state what action it is taking to ensure full routine safeguards activities are carried out at the closed Sellafield facility and, finally, has the Euratom Safeguards Office been given any additional responsibilities following the events of 11 September 2001?

Discrepancies may emerge in bulk handling installations due to differences in the operators and inspectors measurement systems, due to clerical errors or due to process losses. Discrepancies appear frequently, yet they have always been resolved up to now by closer investigations using operating data and source data and by physical verifications of the material involved. Hence, the Commission has no reason to conclude that nuclear material has been diverted from its intended use.

The MOX Demonstration Facility (MDF) has been closed down as a fabrication installation, it is still possession of nuclear materials which remained static and were always under Euratom control. The results of the control were satisfactory as reported.

Concerning the issue of the old Pu-holding-materials, the situation remains unchanged, i.e. the materials remain ‘frozen’ (under seal and/or surveillance). The Commission does not consider under the present circumstances justifiable to request a very significant workload and radiation exposure to facility personnel and inspectors in order to improve their knowledge over this static material. However, normally, whenever such material needs to be handled for other purposes, the inspectors use the opportunity for verifying it.

 
 

Question no 83 by Pat the Cope Gallagher (H-0220/02)
 Subject: Proposed European health card
 

Will the Commission state what the objectives are for the proposed European Health Card and when it expects it to be introduced and will apply to EU citizens travelling to other EU countries on holiday and will it replace the E111 form?

 
  
 

Further to the proposals made in the Commission's Action Plan on Skills and Mobility(1), the Barcelona European Council decided that a European Health Insurance Card will replace the current paper forms needed for health treatment in another Member State and that the Commission will present a proposal to that effect before the Spring European Council in 2003.

Currently Community Regulation No 1408/71 of the Council of 14 June 1971 on the application of social security schemes to employed persons and their families moving within the Community(2) guarantees that the Member State where a person is insured will be responsible for the health care costs if a person is staying temporarily in another Member State and needs immediate care. In order to prove that this person is legally entitled to such care and that he will be covered by the Member State where he is insured, the latter provides him with a paper form (E 111).

The purpose of the European health insurance card is to transform the European paper forms into an electronic health insurance card, so that the cardholder can claim access to urgent health care in a Member State other than the one where he is insured, which will nevertheless remain responsible for the cost.

The main objective is to cut down on paperwork and red tape, and to facilitate access to urgent health care for citizens travelling around the Community. Such a card will not only contribute to improving free movement and labour mobility within the Union but will also give a strong symbolic signal to citizens. As is the case with the E 111 form, the health card will also be available to Union citizens travelling to other Member States on holiday. The intention is not to create new rights for insured persons, nor to introduce a harmonised health or social security card. Neither is it to create a health card containing information on the holder's health status.

The Commission hopes the necessary decisions can be taken and implemented by 2004.

 
 

(1) COM(2002)72 13.02.2002.
(2) Last consolidated version: OJ L 028 30.01.1997.

 

Question no 84 by Liam Hyland (H-0222/02)
 Subject: Campaigning for the take-off of renewable energy
 

The Commission will recall that in its 1997 White Paper on Energy for the Future: Renewable Sources of Energy it sought to launch a campaign for the take-off of renewables and it promised to send a Communication every two years to the Council and the Parliament evaluating the strategy and recommending a revised strategy, if necessary.

Taking into account the targets of 2010 in the White Paper, does the Commission consider that its campaign has taken off and that real progress is being made to exploit the various renewable energies which are abundantly available in the EU but which, in the past, have been neglected and, in particular, has progress been made in selecting pilot communities, regions, cities and islands which could reasonably aim at 100% supply from renewables, and is the potential, recognised by the Commission, for exploiting wood and agricultural residues including straw being realised together with the potential for biofuels, high yield annual and perennial C4 plants and solid vegetable biomass?

 
  
 

In February 2001, the Commission published its first report(1) on the progress achieved towards the White Paper objectives. This report concludes that further efforts at the Community level and in the national policies of the Member States will be necessary to achieve the objectives of the White Paper, i.e. a 12% Renewable Energy Source (RES) contribution to the Community gross inland energy consumption in 2010.

The contribution of RES to Community gross inland energy consumption (source Eurostat) has increased from 5% on 1989 to 6% in 1998.

Concerning the integration of renewable energies in 100 communities referred to by the Honourable Member, the Commission concluded in its progress report that the specific objective of “100 communities aimed at 100% of RES supply “ would certainly be reached in 2003. Renewable Energy (RE) Partnerships, signed with such communities, are progressing rapidly and more than 100 potential candidates have been identified.

The Honourable Member highlights the need for recognising the potential of bioenergy. The Commission’s progress report underlines that bioenergy has become an important element of energy, environment and agriculture policy because of its contribution to security of supply. The biomass sector, including the production of heat, fuels and/or electricity from agriculture, industry and municipal wastes, has shown an increase of 13.5% in the Community during the period 1995-1998. In 1999, Agenda 2000 reforms further encouraged diversification of rural economy, providing aids for investments in RES exploitation and afforestation of agricultural land. In addition, the prolongation of set-aside policy offered a potential of 3.9 million hectares (ha) agricultural land to non-food production at preferential conditions, which resulted in the campaign of 1999-2000 in a use of 1.1 million ha of set-aside land for non-food cultures.

In order for renewable energy technologies to move fully into the mainstream of Community energy supplies (mass markets), and to compete in the single European Energy Market, different legislative frameworks should be amended to provide a more “level playing field”. This is why the Commission has recently launched a major programme of legislative initiatives (Directives) in the renewable energy sector.

The European Commission adopted on 7 November 2001 a legislative package as a follow-up to its recent White Paper on Transport Policy. The package contains a Communication (COM(2001) 547 final) including two proposals for Directives.

The Communication puts forward a concrete action plan for the promotion of alternative fuels and biofuels in road transport by concentrating policy efforts on the promotion of biofuels, natural gas and hydrogen.

Both proposals for Directives aim at promoting the use of biofuels for transport. The first proposal requires that an increasing proportion of all diesel and gasoline sold in the Member States be biofuel, starting with 2% in 2005. The second proposal creates a European-wide framework allowing Member States to apply reduced tax rates in favour of biofuels.

Moreover, the promotion of renewables in the Community is one of the key measures needed for reaching the Union Kyoto target of reducing its greenhouse gas emissions by 8% by 2010 in relation to the 1990 level. The Commission is actively pushing renewables as an important component of its Climate Change strategy.

In the context of the European Climate Change Programme launched in June 2000 and based on a Commission Communication(2), renewables have played a fundamental role. The promotion of renewable energy is also an important element in the forthcoming Public Awareness Campaign and Campaign for Take-off foreseen by the Commission Communication on the implementation of the first phase of the European Climate Change Programme(3).

 
 

(1) COM(2001) 69 final of 16.02.2001.
(2) EU policies and measures to reduce greenhouse gas emissions: Towards a European Climate Change Programme (ECCP), COM(2000)88 final of 8.03.2000.
(3) COM(2001)580 final of 23.10.2001.

 Subject: Somport tunnel
 

The Somport tunnel was begun in January 1994, the ERDF providing 85% of the funding. Since then, construction work has continued and the tunnel is due to open to light vehicles during the first half of 2002.

For reasons unknown it would appear that the opening of the tunnel has been repeatedly delayed and postponed. Furthermore, although work on the road leading to the tunnel on the Spanish side is complete, this is not the case on the French side.

Is the Commission aware of the delay in the opening of the tunnel? If so, does it know what the causes are of that delay, which is restricting the free movement of persons and goods?

 
  
 

The works and studies of the cross-border project mentioned by the Honourable Member were earmarked for Cohesion Fund and Trans-European Networks (TEN-Transport) instrument assistance in 1994 and 1990/1991 respectively. The beneficiaries were Spain and France.

The Commission is aware of the delay on the conclusion of the construction works of the road tunnel and wishes to recall that it also co-finances part of the works on the access roads on the French side of this important project.

Although the Commission understands that parts of this delay are due to objective reasons (i.e. stricter safety rules in tunnels and work in sensitive environmental areas) in France, it would like to point out that the completion of this link is vital to the improvement of interconnections between Spain and France and to the functioning of the internal market.

According to the last information given by the French authorities to the Commission, the opening of the tunnel is going to take place by the end of 2002, after the necessary safety trials have taken place to ensure a high level of safety for all users.

 

Question no 86 by Jan Andersson (H-0228/02)
 Subject: Low aid targets
 

The conclusions to the Barcelona Summit state that the collective goal for the EU Member States is to reach an average of '0.39% ODA/GNI by 2006' and that the Member States 'will in any case strive to reach, within their respective budget allocation processes, at least 0.33% ODA/GNI by 2006'. This can only be regarded as a feeble ambition, particularly in relation to the UN's target of 0.7% ODA/GNI.

In the light of these poor results, does the Commission have any practical strategy for raising the Union's level of ambition in respect of aid to enable the UN target of 0.7% ODA/GNI to be reached as soon as possible?

 
  
 

The Commission considers the commitments on Overseas Development Aid (ODA) levels taken by the Member States at the Barcelona Summit to be a major achievement, because for the first time in a decade the "race to the bottom" as regards ODA has been replaced by a "race to the top". As clearly spelled out by the Council Conclusions, the 0.33% is to be considered as the first significant step towards the achievement of the 0.7% target, to which all Member States remain committed.

The Commission also draws the attention of the Honourable Member to the fact that the Barcelona Conclusions represent the first real collective European commitment on ODA levels and that the Treaty on European Union (TEU) does not confer competence on the Community in this matter.

In that light, the Commission does not consider the additional European ODA of EUR 20 billion to be generated by 2006 through implementation of the Barcelona Conclusions as a “poor result”, nor was it received as such by developing countries and multilateral development agencies at the United Nations (UN) Conference on Financing for Development in Monterrey (18-22 March 2002).

 

Question no 87 by Olle Schmidt (H-0229/02)
 Subject: EU support for WADA
 

The Swedish media report that the EU has decided to withdraw its support of some EUR 3.3 million to the World Anti Doping Agency (WADA) for budgetary reasons.

WADA is sport's weapon in the fight against drug-use. In the light of all the unfortunate reports concerning drug-use among participants at the Winter Olympics in Salt Lake City, this fight is more important than ever. In order to get to grips with doping, we must fight it together. Without the EU's support, WADA's future is uncertain.

The EU was to contribute approximately one sixth of WADA's total budget of just over EUR 20 million. The International Olympic Committee (IOC) is paying its share of EUR 10 million.

Is it true that the EU will not be paying its share of support to WADA and, if so, what other anti-doping measures does the Commission plan to take?

 
  
 

Before answering the question on financing, it should be recalled that the World Anti-Doping Agency is a private-law organisation, whose role is to ensure the international coordination of the method utilised in combating doping in sport. Nonetheless, prime responsibility for the fight against doping lies with the sporting organisations, cooperating fully in certain areas with the national governments.

It is the case that the Commission is not considering making a contribution to financing the Agency’s operating budget. The reasons for this have been explained on several occasions. The main problem lies in the fact that the Commission would be committing itself to paying, on an annual basis, 18% of the Agency’s budget, decided on by two-thirds majority vote of the Foundation Board, where the Community is in a minority. This means that this body could unilaterally determine the amount to be funded by the Community, which would be contrary to the budgetary procedures in force within the Community.

The Commission is, nevertheless, willing to collaborate with the Agency on specific projects. For example, in 2001, three projects proposed by the Agency were funded by the Commission, amounting to EUR 2 million overall.

 

Question no 88 by Helena Torres Marques (H-0230/02)
 Subject: Regulation (EC) 2560/2001 on cross-border payments in euros
 

European Parliament and Council Regulation (EC) 2560/2001(1) on cross-border payments in euros was published on 28 December 2001.

Pursuant to that legislation (which is directly applicable under the Member States’ internal legal systems), cash withdrawals from automatic teller machines and credit- or debit-card payments up to a value of EUR 12 500 in any euro-zone country will, with effect from 1 July 2002, be effected under the same terms and conditions as in the country in which the card was issued. With effect from 1 January 2003 the same will apply to bank transfers in the euro zone.

Pursuant to Article 7 of the Regulation, compliance with the provisions thereof shall be guaranteed by effective, proportionate and deterrent sanctions.

However, most Europeans are unaware that the regulation exists, although it will enable significant saving to be made (particularly in the case of tourists) from next summer onwards.

What action is the Commission taking (or will it take) in order to protect consumers’ rights by making information available and ensuring that the banking sector in the euro zone complies with the regulation?

 
  
 

Regulation 2560/2001 on cross-border payments in euro was adopted on 19 December 2001 and some of its provisions entered into force on 31 December 2001. With effect from 1 July 2002, charges for the withdrawal of euro from automatic cash dispensers and for the use in euro of payment cards must be the same for transactions within a country and across borders. Up until this date, banks and payment card companies may deduct different charges for cross-border transactions in euro.

This Regulation is marked by its simplicity. The equal-tariff rule can be easily explained to tourists using a payment or withdrawal card in automatic cash dispensers. The Commission is therefore persuaded that consumer organisations and the media will circulate this information during June 2002. It will itself be circulating information in the form of a press release during this period.

As regards penalties, the principles of Community law establish this as a matter in which the Member States are competent, under Article 10 of the Treaty establishing the European Community. That is why, in January 2002, the Commission drew the attention of government experts on payments to the obligations of the national authorities arising from the adoption of the Regulation.

The introduction, on 1 January 2002, of euro notes and coins, was, for the public, the first step in the creation of a Single Payment Area. The second step will be the alignment of tariffs in the use of cards and automatic cash dispensers on 1 July 2002.

 
 

(1) OJ L 344, 28.12.2001, p. 13

 

Question no 89 by John Walls Cushnahan (H-0232/02)
 Subject: Airport departure taxes
 

Would the Commission accept that the levying of departure taxes at EU airports is a disincentive to intra-EU travel, especially when applied to the return leg of tickets which are normally held by travellers who are not citizens of the levying country, and therefore constitutes an impediment to the right to free movement?

The question of free movement of persons has been brought numerous times before the Court of Justice. On those occasions, the Court has examined whether specific national measures introduce a discriminatory treatment based on the nationality of citizens of the Community or on discrimination between citizens and non-citizens(1) 13.

As to whether airport taxes constitute an impediment to the free movement of services, this matter too has been the subject of litigation cases brought before the Court of Justice. In two recent cases(2) 14, it has been examined whether the imposition of airport taxes irrespective of the nationality of the passengers but depending on their destination would infringe the freedom to provide services. In both cases the Court has held that “a measure that makes the provision of cross-border services more onerous than that of comparable domestic services amounts to a restriction of the freedom to provide services” 15.

The Commission also wishes to point out that on the basis of jurisprudence from the Court of Justice it has been established that national measures which, even if they are not discriminatory, are liable to hinder or make less attractive the exercise of fundamental freedoms guaranteed by the EC Treaty, are incompatible with Community law unless they are justified by imperative requirements in the general interest, are suitable for securing the attainment of the objective which they pursue, and do not go beyond that is necessary in order to attain it.

 
 
 

(1)13 In particular Case C-459/99 MRAX versus Kingdom of Belgium.
(2)14 Commission versus Italy (Case C-447/99) and Commission versus Portugal (Case C-70/99).

 

Question no 90 by Olivier Dupuis (H-0234/02)
 Subject: Voice of Europe
 

On 13 December 2001, the European Parliament adopted the budget of the European Union for the financial year 2002.

One of its priorities was the establishment of a radio station entitled: Voice of Europe, to be financed from Item B7-7010 – Development and consolidation of democracy and the rule of law – Respect for human rights and fundamental freedoms. Ever since it gave a first reading to the budget on 25 October 2001, the European Parliament has been expecting the Commission to do its utmost to ensure that this new project got off the ground as rapidly as possible.

However, the Commission has still not yet issued a call for proposals, although some five months have elapsed since it was informed of this priority undertaking.

When does the Commission intend to take action on this priority issue established by the European Parliament? What logistical and human resources will the Commission make available so as to ensure that this project is implemented rapidly and effectively in those countries suffering from a significant democratic deficit?

 
  
 

In May 2001, the Commission adopted a communication on the EU’s role in promoting human rights and democratisation in third countries. The basis for this communication is the Commission’s policy on the use to which Chapter B7-7 (European Initiative for Democracy and Human Rights) funds are put; it proposes a new approach and defines thematic priorities and a number of countries to be targeted. In the conclusions of the General Affairs Council in June 2001, the Council gave a favourable verdict on this document.

In order to implement these political guidelines, the Commission, in December 2001, adopted a Programming Document for the European Initiative for Democracy and Human Rights (Chapter B7-7 of the EU Budget), and this document was the subject of informal preliminary discussions by Parliament and the most representative non-governmental organisations in the human rights field.

The Commission notes, with regard to Parliament’s comments on the Budget, that certain of the proposed activities exceed the Budget resources available and do so by a large amount.

In particular, the Commission takes the view that this latter category includes the establishment of a ‘Voice of Europe’ radio station. By way of comparison, ‘Voice of America’ has a total staff of 1 200, with a budget of over USD 131 million (over EUR 148 million) in 2001, which does not include the cost of broadcasting and of other support services. The annual budget of the European Initiative for Democracy and Human Rights is itself only some EUR 100 million.

Under these conditions, the Commission takes the view that if this priority were taken into account, the guidelines sketched out in its communication and the priorities listed in its 2002-2004 Programming Document on Chapter B7-7 might not be respected.

Moreover, if such an initiative is launched without appropriate staff and resources, there is a risk that expectations might not be met.

Question no 94 by María Sornosa Martínez (H-0239/02)
 Subject: Irregularities in the LEADER+ selection process in the Valencian Autonomous Community
 

Within the LAGs, the Valencian regional authorities have included a public involvement prerequisite which automatically disqualifies groups which successfully worked with LEADER II. I believe that the public involvement prerequisite which appears in the Orden de la Consellería (Valencian Government Law) of 2 November 2000, like all the obligations to which it gives rise, runs counter to the thinking underpinning the Commission Communication to the Member States of 14 April 2000 on the LEADER initiative, and totally undermines the autonomy of the LAGs.

Given that the Commission requires a detailed definition of the entire selection procedure, and that the Member States are obliged to ensure that the procedure is a transparent one, what approaches has the Commission made to the Spanish Government in respect of the LEADER+ selection process anomalies in Valencia? What means are at the Commission’s disposal to rectify the situation?

Joint Answer

The selection of Local Action Groups is the exclusive responsibility of national authorities. The selection criteria described in the programme and the subject of a special study by the Commission at the time of the negotiations must be respected.

By virtue of the impartiality that applies in the selection of Local Action Groups, and with the agreement of the national authorities, the Commission is in favour of a competitive procedure based on objective selection criteria and in accordance with the standards in force in the particular Member State.

It should be remembered that simply having been part of the Leader I and II Local Action Groups is not a reason for automatic inclusion in Leader +. The Commission has always favoured new groups’ being set up.

The problem raised by the honourable Members has been notified to the Spanish authorities so that the points of disagreement might be clarified. In the event of fraud or breaches of Community regulations being discovered, the Commission would contact the relevant services, including the European Anti-Fraud Office if necessary.

Question no 96 by Antonios Trakatellis (H-0241/02)
 Subject: Restructuring of Olympic Airways (OA) and the abandonment of profitable routes operated by Olympic Aviation
 

Following the Commission's decision to launch an inquiry in Greece concerning the misuse of aid to OA, including the loan (February 2002) of EUR 9.5 million to address existing problems, the management of OA has been taking counter-productive decisions and saddling OA - which is already bankrupt - with fresh debts and jeopardising the position of its financially sound subsidiaries. OA's management has decided to abandon profitable routes linking Thessaloniki with European cities and assigned the newly acquired aircraft to domestic low-capacity destinations; there is a danger that this will result in new debts, while at the same time cutting off northern Greece and harming productive enterprises.

Will the Commission say whether it intends, as part of the inquiry into the abuse of state aid and the restructuring of the company, to call for a stop to any decision or action which harms the company and leads OA to bankruptcy and its subsidiaries into debt and to examine the decision to abandon profitable routes operated by Olympic Aviation? What measures does it intend to take to ensure that the rules and guidelines governing state aid and the bailing-out and restructuring of ailing undertakings are respected and that the restructuring of OA makes progress, without harming profitable companies such as Olympic Aviation and Macedonian Airlines?

 
  
 

The Commission wishes to inform the honourable Member that the abandonment of certain routes and the re-assignment of aircraft to others are decisions of a purely commercial nature by the management of Olympic Airways (OA). In the framework of the procedure opened on 6 March 2002 pursuant to Article 88 of the EC Treaty and relating both to the misuse of aid already approved and granted to Olympic Airways by decision of the Commission in 1994 and 1998 and to new illegal aid granted to that company since then, the Commission will be able to carry out a detailed examination of economic and financial developments in the company since 1998, as well as of these decisions by OA’s management. The Commission’s decision to open the above-mentioned procedure was notified on 11 March 2002 to the Greek authorities, who have one month within which to forward their initial observations to the Commission.

As for possible non-compliance both with the restructuring plan covering the period 1998-2002 and with the many conditions imposed by the 1994 decision, if the Commission observes that a Member State is not complying with a conditional or negative decision applying to it, and in particular if there is abuse of the decision, then the Commission may, in accordance with the provisions of the procedural regulations relating to state aid(1), immediately contact the Court of Justice, and this in accordance with Article 88(2) of the Treaty establishing the European Community.

With regard to the payment of new forms of aid, if the Commission observes, in the course of its inquiry, that the Greek state is continuing to make illegal forms of aid available to OA, it may take a decision known as a ‘suspension injunction’, enjoining the Member State to suspend the payment of any illegal aid until such time as the Commission has ruled on the compatibility of this aid with the Common Market.

In the same way, the Commission may take a decision known as a ‘recovery injunction’, enjoining the Member State provisionally to recover any aid paid illegally until such time as the Commission has ruled on the compatibility of this aid with the Common market, and this on condition that the following criteria are fulfilled:

- there is no doubt, according to an established procedure, that the measure concerned constitutes aid;

- there is an urgent need to take action;

- a competitor is at serious risk of sustaining substantial and irreparable prejudice.

 
 

(1) Council Regulation (EC) No 659/1999 of 22 March 1999 laying down detailed rules for the application of Article 93 of the EC Treaty

 

Question no 97 by Astrid Thors (H-0244/02)
 Subject: State aid to shipping in the Member States
 

The Member States did not succeed in reaching agreement in the 1990s on rules for a common EU shipping register. In its place, the Commission issued rules for aid to shipping which were published in early 1997. Since that point, there has been a continuous trend towards more generous rules for shipping, with various national registers granting tax exemptions.

At the Barcelona Summit, EU leaders agreed that state aid should be reduced. In the light of that 'decision', what is the Commission's view of the trend in aid to shipping in the form allowed by the register rules? Does the Commission have an idea of the amount of direct and indirect state aid paid to shipping over the years since the introduction of the new guidelines, including special schemes for scrapping such as the programme recently approved in Italy? What measures will the Commission take to achieve a balanced development and to comply with the conclusions of the Barcelona Summit concerning shipping? Are there plans to reconsider the subject of an EU shipping register?

 
  
 

As the honourable Member recalls, the Member States did not reach an agreement on establishing a European shipping register, as proposed by the Commission in 1989. Without excluding the possibility, the Commission does not consider this to be the right time, as matters stand, to submit a new proposal along these lines.

State aid to shipping is part of a Community policy designed to safeguard employment in the Community, preserve the Community’s maritime expertise, develop maritime skills and enhance safety, as well as reduce the competitive disadvantage suffered by European operators who, at international level, are obliged to confront operators who, more often than not, are subject to less stringent requirements in fiscal, social and, in particular, safety terms. For these reasons, aid in this sector has been the subject of back-up measures, particularly Community guidelines on state aid to shipping.(1). The Commission takes cognizance of the aid notified in this framework and, if appropriate, gives its agreement. At present, the aforesaid guidelines are in the process of being revised with a view to ensuring better use of these measures. However, their objectives – as expressed in the White Paper on European Transport Policy(2) – remain unchanged.

 
 

(1) OJ C 205 of 5.07.1997
(2) Doc. COM(2001) 370 of 12.09.2001

 

Question no 98 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H-0245/02)
 Subject: Management of solid waste in Greece
 

How does the Commission assess the management of solid waste in Greece, and to what extent does Greece comply with Community legislation?

What measures does it intend to take to ensure that the matter is dealt with more effectively?

 
  
 

From the perspective of Community legislation on waste management, it is important to underline that Directive 75/442/EEC of 15 July 1975 on waste as amended(1) stipulates a number of key obligations for Member State in connection with the management of waste. Specifically, Article 4 requires Member States to take the necessary measures to ensure that waste is recovered or disposed of without endangering human health and without using processes or methods which could harm the environment. Member States are required under that provision to take the necessary measures to prohibit the abandonment, dumping or uncontrolled disposal of waste. In addition, Article 8 requires Member States to ensure that any holder of waste has it handled by a private or public waste collector or recovers or disposes it himself in accordance with the directive. Furthermore, Article 9 requires that waste disposal operators must obtain a permit from the relevant national competent authority. In addition to Directive 75/442/EEC, Member States must comply with the provisions set out in Directive 1999/31/EC of 26 April 1999(2) on the landfill of waste and Directive 91/689/EEC of 12 December 1991(3) on hazardous waste. Individual Member States are responsible for implementing these Community obligations.

Following on from the ‘Kouroupitos’ judgment(4), in which Greece was required to pay a daily penalty of EUR 20 000 from the Court of Justice under Article 228 of the EC Treaty in respect of non-compliance with its previous judgment(5) concerning the failure by Greece to respect the basic safety requirements of Article 4 of Directive 75/442/EEC on waste in relation to an illegal rubbish tip in the area of Chania Crete, the Commission continues to be concerned about the state of waste management in Greece, in particular in relation to disposal operations. The Commission is following up a number of recent complaints it has received concerning waste disposal activities in that country. In its national waste management plan of June 2000, Greece estimates that for 1999 only 51.3% of municipal waste disposal went to what it terms ‘sanitary landfill sites’, the rest going to ‘ordinary tips’ which according to the plan only meet ‘sanitary landfill site’ specifications partly or not at all. It is not clear to what extent the definitions ‘sanitary landfill site’ and ‘ordinary tip’ fulfil the criteria set by the Landfill Directive 1999/31/EC for hazardous, non-hazardous and inert waste landfills. The plan sets out a long-term strategy for future development of ‘sanitary landfill sites’ to service municipal waste disposal as well as an objective to phase-out and restore uncontrolled tips. Whereas Directive 1999/31/EC on landfill of waste had to be transposed into national law by 16 July 2001, Greece has failed to notify the Commission of its transposition legislation and the Commission has already raised this matter with the Greek authorities.

If the Honourable Member or other person has any evidence to the effect that specific Community obligations have been infringed, it is open to them to file a formal complaint with the Commission.

 
 

(1) OJ L 194, 25.07.1975, as amended by Council Directive 91/156/EEC (OJ L 78, 26.03.1991) and Commission Decision 96/350/EC (OJ L 135, 6.06.1996)
(2) OJ L 182, 16.07.1999
(3) OJ L 377, 31.12. 1991
(4) Case C-387/97 Commission v Greece 2000 ECR I-5047
(5) Case C-45/91 Commission v Greece 1992 ECR I-2509

 

Question no 99 by Caroline Lucas (H-0248/02)
 Subject: Private Finance Initiatives (PFIs)
 

Is the Commission aware of the PFI approach in the UK and does the Commission consider it to be in line with existing public procurement legislation? Furthermore, does the Commission judge that such practices are in line with the Commission's proposal for a new directive on the coordination of procedures for the award of public supply contracts, public service contracts and public work contracts (COM(2000)0275/final(1)), particularly with regard to Articles 1.7 and 32? Finally, what would the Commission's evaluation be of the consequences were the Directive to be amended according to the amendments passed by the European Parliament, in particular Amendment No 24?

 
  
 

The Commission would like to inform the Honourable Member that it is aware of the United Kingdom’s private finance initiative (PFI). PFI is a broad concept covering a multitude of arrangements where the public sector contracts to procure services using private sector management skills and private finance. The exact legal and economic make-up of a given PFI transaction will determine the extent to which the matter is regulated by public procurement legislation. Thus, a given PFI transaction may, for example, constitute a public contract, a works concession or a services concession, and will be subject to the appropriate each time provisions of Community law. In other words, PFI projects have to be considered on a case by case basis in order to determine whether they are covered by, and in conformity with, Community public procurement legislation.

As regards the effects that the recent proposal for a new public procurement directive might have on PFI projects, the Commission would like to mention the inclusion of a new buying technique known as the competitive dialogue designed to allow a degree of flexibility in the award of particularly complex contracts. The Commission considers that the competitive dialogue may be an appropriate framework for, among other things, the award of complex PFI public contracts.

On the contrary, the Commission is not aware of any general practices concerning the use of framework agreements, as provided for in Article 1.7 and Article 32 of the proposal, in PFI contracts.

The Commission would also like to comment on the Parliament’s Amendment No 24. It has already indicated to the Parliament that it would be prepared to accept this amendment in principle if it is modified so as to clarify that several contracting authorities may enter into the same framework agreement at the same time.

 
 

(1) OJ C 29 E 30.1.2001, p. 11.

 

Question no 100 by Carlos Carnero González (H-0250/02)
 Subject: Members of Equatorial Guinea's Democratic Opposition Front (FOD) Standing Committee arrested
 

In February 2002 the Government of Equatorial Guinea illegally arrested Faustino Ondo Ebang, a UP militant. During the first few days of March, Felipe Ondo Obiang, Guillermo Nguema Ela and Emilio Ndong Biyogo – all members of the FOD’s Standing Committee – were also illegally arrested. In addition to these most recent arrests carried out in Malabo by a group of so-called security officials (headed by Julián Ondo Nkum), approximately 40 other illegal arrests have been carried out in various towns within Equatorial Guinea. Furthermore, the government has threatened to declare illegal the CPDS, one of the main opposition parties.

Is the Commission aware of these facts? What action is it intending to take with a view to ensuring that a transition to democracy finally gets under way and that free presidential elections are held? In what way is the Commission monitoring the undertakings to that effect which President Obiang gave Mr Prodi in 2000?

 
  
 

The Commission is aware of the events mentioned and is carefully monitoring the development of the situation on the ground.

The Commission is taking part in the European Union’s strengthened political dialogue with Equatorial Guinea on the questions of democratisation, strengthening of the rule of law and respect for human rights.

This dialogue follows the principles of the Cotonou Agreement and is taking place in a rapidly developing socio-economic context. Its aim is radically to change attitudes and habitual responses acquired over decades of secrecy and obscurantism. This change can only be effected in the long term and is all the more difficult inasmuch as there is now a huge amount at stake economically in the country. Indeed, oil resources are constantly on the increase, and foreign aid is now of relatively marginal importance.

In this context, the Commission has decided to fund a project aimed at strengthening the rule of law, enhancing respect for human rights and promoting the emergence of an independent civil society. While bearing in mind that it is the government of Equatorial Guinea that is exclusively responsible for organising any presidential election, the Commission has reiterated its concern that all elections should be free and transparent. At the government’s request, the Commission could lend technical support to the elections.

Both directly and through the reports of the Special Representative of the United Nations’ Commission on Human Rights, the European Commission is monitoring the development of the human rights situation, as well as the commitments made by President Obiang. During his last visit, the Special Representative observed that a start had been made on taking initiatives to comply with the recommendations made in the resolution by the United Nations’ Commission on Human Rights.

 

Question no 101 by Richard Howitt (H-0252/02)
 Subject: High-Level Group of Company Law Experts
 

Does the Commission intend to instruct the High-Level Group of Company Law Experts to put forward specific proposals on Corporate Governance and wider stakeholder involvement? What initiatives for action in this field are currently being discussed by the Commission and its partners?

 
  
 

The 1999 Financial Services Action Plan announced that the commission would launch a comparative review of the corporate governance practices existing in Member States. The external consultant in charge of the study has just delivered its final report to the Commission.

The study, which provides a comprehensive picture on the existing codes, their main provisions and the extent to which they are enforced, will be used as discussion material within the High Level Group (HLG) of Company Law Experts appointed by the Commission last September.

The HLG will organise a wide internet based consultation over the months of April and May, and should deliver its report this summer. The report will contain recommendations as to the way forward with regard to several company law themes, including corporate governance.

The Commission does not intend to instruct the HLG to bring forward specific proposals on wider stakeholder involvement. It belongs to the HLG to determine the nature, the scope and the contents of the recommendations it considers desirable in the area of corporate governance, in the light of the views which interested parties will have expressed in the consultation process.

 

Question no 102 by Inger Schörling (H-0253/02)
 Subject: Conflict of interests for former Commission official
 

During the March part-session, Commissioner Margot Wallström admitted that the former head of the Environment DG, Jim Currie, had accepted a directorship with British Nuclear Fuels Limited (BNFL) without informing the Commission's personnel department in advance. Given the risk that Jim Currie might be able to exploit his knowledge of the Commission's working methods to restrict the Commission's scope for monitoring BNFL, the personnel DG sent him a letter pointing out which rules apply to him as a former Commission official (Articles 16 and 17 of the Staff Regulation).

Has the Commission received a reply to its letter to Jim Currie and will its contents be made public? What options for taking measures does the Commission have if Jim Currie retains his post despite the Commission not authorising him to do so? What are the repercussions for a former official who does not give notice of new duties even though they could result in conflicts of interests? Does the Commission have any idea how many former officials take up duties that could give rise to suspicion of a conflict of interests and how often has the Commission been compelled to take action?

 
  
 

The Commission has received a reply to its letter to the former official in question. Since this is a personnel matter it is not appropriate, or in keeping with best practice, for the contents to be made public. The Commission can inform the Honourable Member, however, that the former official has indicated that he has not yet accepted the post in question.

The Commission is studying the file and relevant considerations. It is not possible at this stage, therefore, to give any indication of the decision that the Commission will take.

The established convention of the Commission in cases of this kind is that, in the event that the Commission does not give its authorisation, the Commission expects a former official not to take up the post. If, despite that, the post is taken up the Commission has the option of starting disciplinary measures against the former official concerned.

In a case where a former official does not give notice of new duties, and where the Commission becomes aware of this, the ex-official is reminded of his or her duties and asked to clarify to the Commission the nature of the post that has been accepted. In the case of a conflict of interest, he or she could be asked to stop his or her job immediately. If that is not done, the ex-official could be subject to disciplinary measures.

Generally, ex-officials request permission before undertaking any new activity. To the knowledge of the Commission, based on the last five years, former officials take up duties in about 30 cases per year.

Within the last five years, after examination, the permission has been totally refused in one case, partially refused in three cases, conditionally accepted in three cases, and granted in all other cases.

In its proposal for modification of the Staff Regulations in the context of Administrative Reform, the Commission proposes to strengthen Article 16 of the Staff Regulations by placing an obligation on a former official to notify the Institution if he or she proposes to take up a professional activity within two years of termination of service. Similarly, Article 17 has been strengthened in respect of the divulging of information not yet in the public domain.

 

Question no 103 by Rodi Kratsa-Tsagaropoulou (H-0256/02)
 Subject: Official languages and the future of Europe
 

As part of its general language policy, the Commission is adopting the principle of maintaining the 'language range' (the Socrates and Leonardo Da Vinci programmes on multilingualism and vocational training) in the work of the Community institutions and recognises it as an indispensable element of European integration. In its communication in the White Paper on European Governance COM(2001)0428/fin.(1) it stresses that European institutions should continue working under the present system, using eleven official languages.

Will it include the languages of the candidate countries in this framework? How does it view the problem of multilingualism, given the need for Europeans to understand each other and work together? Has it undertaken any studies on effective policies in this area? Does it intend to place this matter on the agenda of the debate on the future of Europe?

 
  
 

As the Honourable Member points out, the Commission confirmed in its White Paper on Governance (point III.1.) that it is essential that communication between the institutions and the citizens takes place in all of the official languages. This practice is legally guaranteed by the EC Treaty (Article 21) and the "language regime" laid down in Regulation 1/58 of the Council, which stipulates that all official languages are also working languages of the institutions(2). Enlargement will raise the number of the official and working languages, which in turn calls for more efficient solutions for translation and interpretation. The translation and interpretation services of the institutions of the Union are currently undertaking efforts in that direction. Any other solution would imply a modification of the Treaties. The Commission has no indication on whether this will be raised in the Convention on the future of Europe.

 
 

(1) OJ C 287, 12.10.2001, p.1.
(2) OJ 17 6.10.1958 (Article 1, page 385)

 

Question no 104 by Nelly Maes (H-0262/02)
 Subject: Implementation of 1999 Language Law in Slovakia
 

Commissioner Verheugen confirmed to the author of this question last November that the implementation of the 1999 Language Law in Slovakia was not entirely satisfactory and should be closely monitored. Following the publication of the results of the 2001 census:

Does the Commission have the results of the census, and, if so, is it able, on the basis of these data, to estimate how many communities in Slovakia have minority groups representing more than 20% of their population? If this is the case, could the Commission provide us with these data, and what steps does the Commission intend to take to further assess the shortcomings previously noted with regard to the implementation of the Language Law in such communities?

If it does not have such data at its disposal, does it intend to request them in order to enable a thorough assessment to be made of the implementation of the 1999 Language Law, and when will the results of such an assessment be submitted to Members of the European Parliament?

 
  
 

In its last Regular Report of November 2001 on Slovakia(1), the Commission noted the substantial efforts made by the Slovak Government regarding national minorities and notably the ratification by Slovakia of the European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages applying to all minority languages in the country in municipalities with over 20% of any of those minorities. The Commission noted also that concerning the implementation of the Law on the use of Minority Languages, there continues to be limited availability of concrete data. It confirmed the impression noted in last year’s report that in many areas national minorities do not make use of the rights granted under the law due to lack of valid information.

In preparation of its evaluation in its next Regular Report on Slovakia to be published in October 2002, the Commission is closely monitoring this question and using all available information, including monitoring reports on the application of the European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages. In October 2001, the Slovak statistical office released the Slovak national census of inhabitants, houses and apartments. The Commission is analysing this very substantial data to see how it could be a valuable source of information for its monitoring exercise.

 
 

(1) COM(2001)700 final
